SHERIFF FARLEY'S SLAYER ESCAPES
THE FURY OF THE PEOPLE OF SALINAS
Officers Smuggle Suesser
From the Scene of His
Atrocious Crime to
San Jose.
SALINAS. Sept. 19.âSa
linas is suffering to-day a
reaction from a night of
hysteria. People of the town
are gathered on every street cor
ner discussing in excitement the
tragic scenes of last night when
Henry R. Farley, the Sheriff of
Monterey County, was murdered
by an infuriated drunken wretch
who gave in murder and arson
a terrible climax to a worthless
life.
Business is practically sus
pended. With a fascination of
horror hundreds of men and
women are congregated at the
scene of the tragedy. Crowds
are assembled at the County Jail
to tell their regret that the mur
derer of the Sheriff did not re
ceive summary and desperate
justice for his crime.
Threats are made on even
hand that the criminal will never
be tried by process of law in the
county. Never, perhaps, in the
history of Monterey has a crime
so agitated the people, and armed
men are everywhere ready to re
venge the death of one who
seems to have won universal re
spect and good will. The mur
derer has been hastened out of
the county and smuggled to San
Jose, where he will remain until
people <>f Salinas have calmed
themseivrs sufficiently to permit
him to have the chance that the
law gives him for his life.
The mourning relatives of the
officer are gathered at his
coffin, and the people of the town
are preparing funeral ceremonies
that will testify their esteem oi
the man who was said to be the
most popular man in the county.
When daylight came this
morning the streets were dotted
with armed men prepared for any
desperate encounter. They had
been abroad for hours, and
they had watched with unremit
ting vigilance to lake the life of
the murderer.
It was perhaps an hour after
George Suesser had murdered
Sheriff Farley before the people
of the town knew that the officer
had been shot to death. Suesser.
it will be remembered, had
nursed his rage against Delaney
for hours. Suesser is hardly more
than a boy in years. He is a
worthless, drunken fellow, 22
years of age. He has won a pre
carious living by hunting, being
a dead shot and too lazy to fol
low a regular occupation. He
has been in trouble times without
number. He has been accused of
many minor offenses, but suc
ceeded by some streak of good
fortune from suffering punish
ment for them. Three days ago
he was accused by James 1 >e
laney of having stolen a harness.
The charge angered the young
fellow and he swore to be re
venged. He drank heavily dur
ing the day, and early in the
evening went to the home of De
laney. The old man answered
the call at the door, and when he
came Suesser struck him bru
tally and heavily with an iron bar.
Delaney escaped serious injury
through the assistance of his fam
ily, and Suesser reeled away
vowing that he would return and
kill the household.
As he left he drew his pistol
and fired several shots ,at the
house. Fortunately, he did no
damage, but his desperate nature
was aroused. He leaped a fence
and in drunken viciousness set
fire to a haystack owned by De
laney. In a moment the flames

THE FURY OF THE PEOPLE OF SALINAS
shot high into the air. A fire in
the very center of the town nat
urally created great excitement.
The alarm drew hundreds to the
scene, and in a few minutes the
neighboring streets were black
with people. The sound of the
pistol shots and the light of the
flames drew Constable Charles
Allen to the place. He saw the
drunken criminal just as he was
leaving the field. The officer
commanded Suesser to surren
der, and the reply was two pistol
bullets which went wide of their
mark.
After firing Suesser fled and
the officer gave chase. The young
man was too fleet of foot, how
i cevr, and reached his home,
; where he secured a shotgun. A
few minutes later he met the man
for whose murder he must now
answer with his life.
The alarms and the excitement
j had attracted the attention of
; Sheriff Farley, who had just re
! turned from the country and was
: dining in a downtown restaurant
; with ex-District Attorney P. E.
Zabala. They determined to
visit the scene of the fire, and on
the way they met Constable Al
len. Pie told them the story *ofj
Suesser's offense, and Sheriff;
Farley decided to arrest the of- j
fender. The officer had almost !
I reached Suesser's home when he
saw the young man. Farley ,
called upon the young fellow to '
surrender. The reply was a vol- i
; Icy of oaths.
Suesser was armed with a shot
gun and leveled it at Farley.
There was a flash and a report,
1 and the Sheriff dropped to the i
: ground mortally wounded. The
i murderer aimed again and fired,
but the trigger simply snapped.
When he saw what he had done
Suesser fled back to his home.
He rushed for a knife and slashed I
his throat, but did not inflict a
I fatal wound.

been carried to a neighboring
house, where he died shortly
after without regaining con
sciousness. The news of his
death spread like wildfire.
Officers were summoned and
they surrounded Suesser's
home. People flocked from their
Pictorial Storu of the Killing of Sheriff Farleu
at Salinas.
houses and hastened to the scene
of the crime. As they learned
the circumstances of the murder
they hurried back to their homes
to secure arms. Gunmakers
threw open their establishments
and told all that wished to arm
themselves and prepare to cap
ture the murderer. The town
had gone wild with rage over the
brutal crime of the drunken ras
cal.
It was not long before hun
dreds had congregated at the
Suesser house ready to hang the
murderer. Meanwhile the offi
cers were seeking for him. After
cutting his throat he had sought
refuge in the cellar and had
wedged himself between the
foundation and the ground. After
diligent search he was discov
ered. A board was wrenched
from the side of the house and
he was found blood-stained, chat
tering with fear and weak from
loss of blood. The muzzle of a
shotgun was placed at his head
and he was ordered to come from
his hiding-place. He did so, and
heard the angry roar of the
! throng of men who had sur
! rounded his home and were de
manding his life. He begged the
officers to kill him, but they se
cured a buggy near the house
and by a subterfuge escaped with
their prisoner. They made at
once for the outskirts of the
i town, and it was several moments
before shouts and curses an
j nounced that the crowd knew
i that their prospective prey had
escaped.
The throng then surged
toward the County Jail. News of
the murder had spread to neigh
boring towns, and in Gonzales
and Castroville armed men or-
ganized and hurried to Salinas.
Within an hour the roads were
patrolled by mounted armed men
ready to lynch the murderer.
But the officers were too clever.
They hastened their bleeding
prisoner out toward the Catholic
Cemetery, which is about two
miles from town. One of the of
ficers, James Church, left the
buggy and returned to the city
to endeavor to calm the people.
The other, M. R. Keef, con
tinued with his charge toward
the graveyard. He did not know
what to do. The roads seemed
guarded in every direction. By
taking a circuitous route he
reached the Howard ranch, near
Natividad, however, and there
the murderer rested until early
morning.
But the crowd of armed, rope
equipped men had not left the
jail during the long hours of the
night. They were waiting in
desperate patience for the com
ing of the murderer and his cap
tors. And with all their vigilance
they were outmatched. In the
early morning Suesser was
â  brought back to the town and to
I jail. He was smuggled into the
Sheriff's office, and the officers
I held a consultation. They de
cided that if they were to save
the life of their prisoner they
must get him out of the county.
Angry men were around the jail
and on every street corner. They
had kept their vigil through the
night, and failure had simply in
tensified their purpose.
After planning for nearly an
hour the officers succeeded in
getting a surrey to the rear en
trance of the jail. Suesser was
bundled into it and thrown on
the bottom of the vehicle.
Blankets were thrown over him,
the horses were lashed into a gal
lop, and the mob was cheated.
The officers made their way to
Sargents, where they took the
train for San Jose. All the way
Suesser declared piteously that
he never intended to kill the
Sheriff, who had been his friend.
''I wanted to kill Allen and De
laney," he cried, "and I am sorry
I did not."
After it became known that
Suesser had been taken out of the
county the mob dispersed. But
it has lost none of its bitterness,
and it will fare ill with the mur
derer if he is brought back while
If the Prisoner Is Returned
to Monterey County He
Will Surely Be
Lynched.
I the people of Salinas are in their
! present mood. They demand a
1 speedy revenge for the crime that
i has outraged the entire county.
To-day a stream of people has
passed in and out of the home of
Thomas Renison, where the
body of the dead Sheriff is rest-
ing.
The inquest was held this
morning and the Coroner's jun
charged Suesser with murder.
Coroner Muller, who has as
sumed the duties of Sheriff, has
sworn in five extra deputies to
assist in keeping the peace, but
there is no likelihood of trouble
unless the murderer is brought
back.
The funeral of Sheriff Farley
will take place at 10 o'clock-
Thursday morning. It will be
held under the direction of the
Native Sons and the Odd Fel
lows, of which orders the dead
officer was a prominent member.
SUESSER TALKS OF
HIS MANY CRIMES
SAN JOSE, Sept. 19.â George Suesser.
the murderer of Sheriff Farley, occupies
the cell which formerly held Tiburcio
Vasquez, the notorious Mexican bandit,
in the little tank at the County Jail to
night. The iron bedstead and every
thing has been removed from the cell
so that he cannot kill himself, and Jail
er Rives is keeping close watch on the
prisoner. He lies on a mattress on the
floor, with a couple of blankets over
him.
Suesser was sleeping as soundly and
unconcernedly as an innocent babe to
night when aroused to be interviewed.
He exhibits no remorse for his crime.
Buesser readily consented to sit for his
picture for The Call photographer this
afternoon. He adjusted his attire and
seemed to want to make a good picture.
To-night he quickly answered ques
tions put to him. He said he was sorry
he killed Farley, whom he said was a
friend of his, and that he had no reason
to kill him. The Sheriff did not speak.

"Constable Allen and Delaney have
persecuted me in every way," said
! Suesser. "Four times the former ar
: rested me, and each time I was turned
| loose. Yesterday he wanted to arrest
jme again. This became tiresome, and
i last evening I concluded to even up
I matters. I began drinking, but before
doing so I had decided to kill Delaney
and Allen and then give myself up. I
j went to Delaney's house for the pur
i pose of killing him. If he had come
, out I would have shot him. I did not
hit him with the iron bar, but with my
! fist. I fired three or four shr,-ts through
! the house, but shot too high. That was
! merely to bring Delaney outside. "When
j I ran across Farley I thought it was
Allen, and I shot to kill him. I waa
drunk at the time, but realized what I
was doing. Had Ito do the whole busi
i ness over again I would do the same.
i I felt that if I killed Delaney and Allen
\ and even hung for it I would feel well
i repaid. No, I am not filled with re
' morse nor repentance and don't care
what becomes of me. Yes, I set the
fire?, too."
Suesser has a deep gash around his
throat and is badly powder burned be
tween the eyes. He is not shot in the
stomach, as was at first supposed. In
regard to the wounds he said: "After
the shooting I decided to kill myself. I
went under the house. The muzzle of
a gun I placed to my forehead and
pulled the trigger. It happened that
; there was a blank cartridge in the gun,
which accounts for my failure. The
concussion knocked me down, but I
soon got up. I then grabbed my pocket
! knife and tried to kill myself. It was
j dull and did not reach a vital place.
Before I could do any more damage I
was arrested."
The front of Suesser's shirt is covered
i with blood and he must have bled pro
fusely.
Suesser was taken to the County Jail
'by Deputy Sheriffs Wallace and
Hughes. They arrived at San Jose on
the 2:45 train. Deputy Sheriff Wallace
! gives great credit to Deputies Keefe
: and Nesbitt. They all had a hard time
j to protect Suesser, and although they
i had great animosity toward him, de
j cided to protect him at all .hazards.
AN INQUEST HELD
BY THE CORONER
SALINAS, Sept. 19.â Coroner Muller
held an inquest this morning on the
'. remains of Sheriff Farley. The follow
ing men constituted the jury: H. S.
Ball, F. W. Schmechel. Warren John
son, C. B. Butler, John Tucker. S. J.
Westlake, John Kalar, A. F. Fulton. C.
J. Fulle and J. W. Rowling. After roll
call the jurors were taken to the resi
dence of Thomas Renison. where they
i viewed the remains, and were then
! sworn In. The jurors then returned to
the office of the Coroner, where the in
; quest was proceeded with.
Ex-District Attorney P. E. Zabala
| was the chief witness. He was in the
Sheriff's company from 3:30 continu
ously until the time of the shooting.
They had been out to the Corral de
Troca. Returning about 9:30, they drove
to Mr. Keef's house and left the horse.
They returned to the grill and got sup
per. While there the fire alarm
sounded.
Farley said he would go down town
!to the fire. Witness told him there
would he no use going there and that
the proper place to go was to Suesser's
house if we wanted to find out anything
about the fire. Upon arrival there they
heard loud talking. Suesser's parents
were remonstrating with him. but he
seemed very much excited and said he
was going to kill Delaney. Suesser
went out into the street, continuing his
cursing and threatening. Farley spoke
to him and said: "George, George, be
quiet," but Suesser said: "Stand back.
you . or I'll shoot you. too."
Farley said: "Oh, no, you won't.
George, don't you know who I am?"
and he replied: "Yes, I know who you
are." Farley then urged Suesser to
come back home and behave himself.
Suesser then turned to Farley and
lifting his gun to his shoulder, said:
"Stand back! Stand back! Stand
back!" and upon repeating it the third
time pulled the trigger, the charge
taking effect in Farley's fa<-". When
Suesser fired his father was on the
sidewalk and his mother and brother
were in the yard. They screamed: "Oh,
my God, he has killed an innocent man.
Take him and hang him; he ought to
be killed."
"When we went to the house search
ing for Suesser," continued the witness,
"his folks again reiterated what they
had said and requested us to hang him.
Suesser did not appear to be under the
influence of liquor, but spoke with the
deliberation of one seeking blood. The
Sheriff's pistol was in his pocket. He
had never drawn it."
Marshal Allen testified that he had
the gun of Suesser: that he helped to
capture him from the place of hiding.
When he was pulled out he begged for
a pistol, stating that he would take his
own life.
The Coroner did not deem It neces
sary to introduce further evidence and
gave the case to the jury.
The jury returned the following ver
dict: "We, the Coroner's jury sum
moned to inquire into the cause of the
death of Henry Rled Farley, find that
deceased was shot in the head with
buckshot by George Suesser; that the
shooting was done with intent to kill."2

LEAGUE OF AMERICAN
MUNICIPALITIES MEETS
Members Discuss Matters Appertain-
ing to the Health and Better
Government of Cities.
SYRACUSE, N. Y'., Sept. 19.â third
annual convention of the League of
American Municipalities opened here to
day with 400 delegates in attendance, and
many more are expected to arrive to
morrow. Mayor Maguire of Syracuse
made the address of welcome to the city.
Governor Roosevelt, who was to have de
livered the address of welcome to the
State, telegraphed that he could not be
present. Former Mayor Sewell L. Black
of Columbus, Ohio, president of the
league, responded to the addresses of
welcome.
In his annual address President Black
outlined the purposes of the convention.
Mayor Samuel L. Jones of Toledo made
the principal address of the day. He
spoke upon the subject: "The New
Patriotism, a Golden Rule Government."
Following Mayor Jones, the secretary
and treasurer made their annual reports.
Secretary B. F. Kilklnson stated that,
although the league was but twoyearsof
age, it had on its membership rolls cities
representing about one-sixth the entire
population of the L'nlted States. At the
date of his last report, August 1, 1898,
the league had a membership of seventy
two cities; since then fifty-two cities have
been added to membership. The bureau
received during the year 782 special let
ters of Inquiry, relative to municipal af
fairs. Not having a skilled architect
and a civil engineer, the bureau was un
able to provide all the Information called
for in some cases. The secretary recom
mended that a consulting engineer be re
tained by the league when the revenue of
the organization shall be sufficient to
meet such expense.
The remainder of the afternoon session
was given up to papers and discussions
on "Collection and Disposal of Garbage."
J J. Williams, Mayor of Memphis, Tenn., |
and H. J. Gonden, editor of City Govern
ment, New York, argued in favor of cre- I
mation as the best method of destroying
the waste matter of cities.
"A Plea for the More Comprehensive
Study of Refuse Disposal From a Munici
pal Standpoint," was the subject of an
address delivered by Dr. William C.
Woodward, Health Officer of Washing
ton D C. He urged those present to de
vote their Influences to secure the proper
cleaning of their own city in a compre
hensive manner, and said that then we
should see a lowered mortality and an
Increased vitality and happiness of our
P< This* evening a reception was given by
This evening a reception was given by
Mayor Maguire. the Town Council and
citizens' committee to the visiting dele-
By special request of the delegates.
President Black has invited Colonel
Richard J. Hinton. who is visiting in
Svracuse, to address the convention to
morrow on municipal ownership.

REGISTRY OF SHIPS
IN HAWAII STOPPED
A Proclamation Bearing Upon the
Matter Recently Issued by
President McKinley.
WASHINGTON, Sept. Ift.â The follow-
ing proclamation has been issued:
"By the President of the United States,
executive order:
"In the exercise of the power conferred
upon him by the joint resolution of Con
gress, approved by the President on July
7, IK9*. entitled 'Joint resolution to pro*
vide for annexing the Hawaiian Islands
to the United States.' the President of the
United States hereby directs that the is
sue of registers to vessels by the author
ities-- of Hawaii, entitling such vessels to
all rights and privilege! of Hawaiian ves
sels in the purts of nations or upon the
high seas, shall hereafter cease."
The purpose of this action is supposed
to be the preventing of indiscriminate ad
mission to Hawaiian register of foreign
built ships, which will later on become en
titled to United States register.

TESTIMONY IN
DESIRELLO CASE
Principal Witness Goes
on the Stand. â¢
SAW THE CRIME COMMITTED
JOHN VACCARI TELLS HISSTORY
TO THE JURY.
District Attorney Bullock Does Not
Hope for a Conviction for Mur-
der, but Merely Man-
slaughter.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
REDWOOD CITY, Sept. 19.â The tak- I
ing of testimony In the Desirello murder j
trial commenced this afternoon about 2 j
â¢ '(luck. The entire day up to that time i
was occupied in selecting the jury. In j
all ninety talesmen wore questioned. ]
From their examination it can be seen j
that the strong feeling that existed
against defendant at the time of the kill
ing has materially lessened. Many of
them were disqualified for the reason that
they had formed opinions from . news
paper items read by them. The jury
sworn to try the case is composed of the
following men:
David Barre, Alexander Moore, John
Kerr, Samuel Nash Jr., K. Schubert, K.
Dulac, W. H. Adams. Eugene O'N.eill,
George p. Adair, John Kowell, J. M.
Beckley and r. 1.. Wood.
In Ins opening statement to the jury
District Attorney Bullock told them that
he would not ask for a conviction of
murder, but that from the evidence that
should be introduced he would expect a
verdict of manslaughter. In the first
trial none of the jurors voted for murder,
though nix st. M>d for manslaughter.
The lirst witness called by the prosecu
tion was \v. is. Gilbert, County Surveyor.
The purpose of this testimony was to
identity and explain a drawing and plan
of the territory in the. neigh oor hood of
the White House anil Belli's store at
Colma, where ilie shooting occurred.
The only other witness examined to-day
was John \ accari, the young man who
was driving the team in which young
Johnston was riding at the time he was
shot. Vacccari and a young man by the
name of Henry Jensen, who was also in
the wagon, are the two principal wit
nesses tor the prosecution. Vaccari was
put through a severe cross-examination
by Mr. Walker, who with B. R Kilz
patrick, is conducting the defense.
On his direct examination Jenaon testi
fied that he was working for a man by
the name of Bauman on the lMh of last'
March and on the evening of that day
tie left the ranch of Mr. Bauman in com
pany with James Johnston and v man by
the name of Furger for Belli's store in
Colma to get some hay. When he reached
the switch near the White House he
stopped to let Mr. Kuriier off the wagon,
and there took on young Jensen, the lat
ter accompanying him and Johnston to
the store. Opposite the White House a
man Btepped out toward the wagon and
then back again. He passed the man a
few feet, when a shot was fired. He kept
on, and a s* bond shot was tired. By
this time he had almost reached the store
and was making a long turn when a third
shot was tired, and it was then that
Janus Johnston called out, "I'm shot.",
Johnston jumped from the wagon and ran
to the porch of the store and grasped the
iron bars on the window with one hand.
Tht other hand he held to the back of his
neck. Witness said he jumped from the
wagon and went to the assistance of the
wounded boy. He had no more than
reached him when Desirello ran up. About
the same time defendant's wife appeared
and said to her husband: "My God,
Frank, what have you done?" to which
he replied: "1 don't give a ; 1 shot to
kill. " Witness was about to testify that
he was rebuking Desirello for shooting at
them, but the court ruled the testimony
I out.
Dpsirello. according to thp witness,
! caught Johnston as he was about to fall
and held him In his arms, at the same
time asking his wife for some whisky.
A crowd Soon gathered around and he
was sent to Ocean View for a doctor.
When he returned the boy was dead.
I'pon cross-examination the witness ad
mitted that Desnollo said he shot to kill
the horses and meant the boys no harm.
Up further admitted that at the Coroner's
Inquest and at the preliminary examina
tion he had testified that the defendant
had said he shot to kill. This admission
is in line with the defense made by de
fendant on the first trial.
Henry Jensen will be examined in the
morning. The case will probably go to
the jury some time on Friday.

WORKMEN OF FEDERAL
BUILDING ON A STRIKE
Bricklayers and Stonemasons Object
to Work Done by Non-Union
Men on a Cornerstone.
CHICAGO. Sept. 19*-A formal strike of
bricklayers and stonemasons employed
on the new Federal building was ordered
to-day. The foundation for the corner
stone, which is to be laid by President
McKinley during the fall festival in Oc
tober, was built last Sunday. The work
was done by union masons, but under a
sub-contract from John R. Peirce. who
has the contract for the stone "work of
the Federal building. Few men are af
fected, but unless the strike is settled
it will probably prevent the laying of the
cornerstone.
The strike grievance will be brought
before the Building and Trades Council
Friday night, and unless the disagree
ment between the labor unions and Con
tractor Peirce over the cornerstone,
which it is claimed was cut by non-union
labor, is settled all work on the postoffice
will be stopped.

SUMMONSES ISSUED
FOR CABINET COUNCIL
England and the Transvaal Continue
Making Preparations for War.
LONDON, Sept. 19.â Summonses were !
issued late this afternoon for a Cabinet
council which It is understood will be
I held Friday. This is regarded as the j
1 result of the visit of the Premier, Lord i
1 Salisbury, to London, whither he came |
I this afternoon, remaining at fhe For
eign Office from 4:30 p.. m. to 6:30 p.
Im. Mr. Chamberlain, Secretary of
State for the Colonies, was present in
the building at the same time, and as j
a private doorway connects the offices
it is presumed that the summonses
were the result of their joint delibera
tion.
The lack of all excitement in official
j circles bears evidence to the deliberate
' manner in which the highest officials
j are handling the crisis. Nor is it by
| any means certain that the Cabinet
| council will adopt extreme measures,
in spite of the uncompromising nature
of the Boer reply. This deliberation on
i the part of the Government is inter
! preted not only in London but in other
: European capitals as a desire on the
part of Great Britain to gain time while
the troops are hurrying to Cape Colony.
The Berliner Ntuieste Nachrichten,
j commenting on this to-day, says: "The
present calm is only meant to deceive
the Boers. When' England feels equal
to the task of facing the Boers this
I peaceful tone will give way to a blatant
i cry for war."
Even rumors of a movement of the
' Boers across the Natal border appar
! ently fail to excite the military authori
j ties here, who profess at least not to
fear such a contingency for the present.
In the meantime the movement of
troops proceeds. The Royal West Sus
; sex Regiment left to-day for Malta,
where it will replace the first border
i regiment, which goes by the same
j steamer to Natal.
It is announced this afternoon that
the British first-class protected cruisers
â  Terrible and Powerful have been or
dered to the Cape. They have reliefs
! on board capable of forming a naval
brigade of a thousand men.
Ine steamer Jelunga will take 500
troops bound for Natal to-morrow, and
sly will embark a rifle brigade at the
â¢ island of Crete for the Cape.

ban, Natal, the Premier of the Colony
says that the alarm of the people of
Natal at the unprotected condition of
â  the border towns is quite unwarranted
and that all efforts to incite the Zulus
; have failed.

WIRELESS TELEGRAPHY
Orders Issued for Captain George

WASHINGTON, Sept. 19.â Wireless tel
egraphy is to be given a practical trial in
the vicinity of New York by the sipnal
i corns of the army. Secretary Root to-day
i Issued this order:
"Captain George O. Squier, United
! States Volunteer Signal Corps, will pro
ceed from this city to New York City and
Fire Island, New York, and Navesink.
New Jersey, "ii official business pertain
ing to the signal corps of the army, and
on the completion of this duty will return
to his station In this city via New York
City. N. Y. The travel is necessary for
i public service."
The extent of the experiments Captain
| Squier will make has not been det.r
mined, but he will make all necessan ar-
I rangements and report their completion
at the department. Experiments by the
signal corps with wireless telegraphy up
to this time have been confined to efforts
made to communicate between the depart
ment and Port Meyer, a distance of four
miles and the results have been quite
satisfactory. Better results are expected
1 in the proposed experiments, because of
more favorable conditions under which
they will be made.

PARIS. Sept. 19.â The Council of
Minister? decided to-day to pardon
Dreyfus in principle. The pardon
will take effect in a few days.
Dreyfus has relinquished his appeal for
a reversal of the Judgment of the court
martial.
It is Bald that Dreyfus will be sent
abroad before the promulgation of his
pardon in order to avoid demonstra
tions.
"In principle" is an idiom sometimes
used in semi-official announcements of
forthcoming actions. It Beems to have
but light bearing on the matter, except,
perhaps, that it implies the fulfillment
of various formalities before the par
don is actually issued, therefore quali
fying: the announcement of the pardon
with slight tentativeness. It is not
known yet whether the pardon includes
amnesty.
There is much remark here on the
strange coincidence of the death of M.
Seheurer-Kestner, the first champion of
Dreyfus, and to whom Dreyfus will vir
tually owe his freedom, on the very day
the Cabinet d< cided to pardon the pris
oner.
The decision of the Cabinet to par
don Dreyfus created less interest in
Paris than would the result of a big
horse race. In fact, it may hi> said that
it caused no excitement whatever.
There was n<> riotinp: on the boulevards.

in the evening to gaze upon the huge
rendes outside th^ win

there was not the slightest gathering
or the faintest sign of demonstration,
either for or against Dreyfus.
would think that the fire of the
Dreyfus agitation had burned itself out.

ent's action by a rabid attack
upon President Loubet, but their efforts
were futile. The peaceful crowds that
filled the tables in front of the cafes,

at th< ys who displayed the

violent headlines, but utterly refused
t.p p. Tlv majority did not
even discuss the matter or only made
the passing remark that the decision
was just as had been expected and pre
dicted by every Parisian newspaper
since the Rennes court-martial deliv
ts verdict.

GUERIN AND COMPANIONS
SURRENDER TO THE POLICE
rhe Six Weeks 1 Siege of "Fort Chabrol" Ends in
a Sublimely Ridiculous Style.
PARIS Sept. 20. â M. Guerin surrendered at five minutes to 4 o clock this
morning without firing a shot, and was conveyed to prison. His com
panions, fourteen in number, were allowed to go free. Thus the six
weeks 1 siege ended in sublimely ridiculous style.
Preparations were made to storm "Fort Chabrol." Guerin's strong
hold. At half past 2 o'clock this morning the colonel of the Garde Republi
calne accompanied by two officers, arrived at "Fort Chabrol," followed later
by a body of mounted guards and a company of guards afoot. At a Quar
ter past :, fours' ated cabs arrived with an ambulance to take off the pris
oners and wounded in case of an assault. A company of engineers from
Versatile* a company of firemen and a prefect of police next came. Prep
arations were then made to take the fort after three summones by roll of
drum. Then the surrender was made.
Th.- Government had decided to flood the fort if Querin had refused to
surrender and had a couple of hundred firemen with steam pumps, fire es
capes ."iid miles of hose- All the buildings around were filled with police
and municipal guards. Guerin's house is lower than the surrounding edi
fices and the firemen were placed on upper stories of the latter, overlook
ing the fort, prepared to turn on the water as soon as the signal should be
Kiven. while below, in the street, the engines were snorting as the firemen
fed the furnaces and kept steam at top pressure.
Infantry officers and officers of the municipal guard paced up and down
during the hours of waiting, glancing up at the windows of the fort, from
which, in the dim, gray light of the early morning, could be distinguished
a black flag, hoisted from the second floor by Guerin, and a white sheet
hanging down with the device in black letters, "Vive I'Annee" and "A bas
les traiteurs."
When M. Guerin's companions separated they took coaches and were
driven *ff. Chanteloube joined his parents. Then the troops departed, fol
lowed by the municipal guards and the police, except a few who remained
in the Rue de Chabrol to keep order. An immense crowd lingered to
the end to watch the proceedings and then dispersed with little or no dis
turbance.
THE LATE M. SCHEURER-KESTNER.
DEATH CLAIMS THIS
DREYFUS CHAMPION
PARTS. Sept. 19. â M. Seheurer-Kestner. former vice president of the
Senate and a champion of Dreyfus, is dead. M. Scheurer-Kestner's
death was sudden. He had been ill during the last few days, but it
was not thought that his illness would prove fatal.
M. Auguste Seheurer-Kestner, the former vice president of the
French Senate and foremost champion of the cause of Dreyfus, was born
February 11. 1833, at Mulhouse, Alsace, from which place the Dreyfus fam
ily also hailed. He was president of the Chemical Society of Paris and
was a prominent member of a number of other societies. He was political
director of the Republique Francaise from 1579 to 1884, and was a Repub
lican in politics. He took a great interest in the improvement of the work
ing classes, and in I^." founded a co-operative, society for their benefit. His
republican ideas in I*>'> 7 caused him to be sentenced to four months' impris
onment and 2000 francs' fine. He was elected to the National Assembly in
1871, and in 1875 he was first elected to the Senate, of which he afterward
became one of thÂ»- secretaries and eventually one of the vice presidents. He
was defeated for re-election as vice president January 13, IS9S. He has
written extensively on chemistry.
LONDON, Sept. lfl.â The afternoon newspapers comment chiefly upon
the death of M. Seheurer-Kestner, comparing its dramatic features with the
deaths of Nelson and Wolfe at the moment of victory. M. Seheurer-Kestner
had been suffering for two weeks from typhoid fever.
now be set free and be able to give
lectuns in England and America on the
secrets of the iron safe in the major
bureaux."

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL., WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 20. 1899.
CLOSE CALL
OF TROOPS ON
MORGAN CITY
Transport Was Beached Just
as She Was About to
Go Down.
BsecUU IMsivitch to The fall.
YOKOHAMA, Sept. 8, via Victoria,
B. C, Sept. 19.â Interesting details are
at hand of the wreck of th.-> American
transport Morgan City in the inland
i sea of Japan. It appears that it came
very near being an appalling catas
trophe.
Striking upon a reef at 3 o'clock in
. the morning the ship remained fast un
â  til daylight, when by adopting the ex
pedient of rushing the men alternately
; from one side to the other the ship was
j rolled from the rocks into deep water,
where she at once began to fill rapidly.
She headf^l for the shore of the island
of Inoshima, several miles distant,
sinking perceptibly with every yard of
progress. She reached the beach barely
; a minute before otherwise going under.
â  The discipline of the men was admir
; able and all were gotten ashore in
safety.
The seamanship shown appears not
i to have been so admirable, as no pre
! cautions were taken to make the bow
( fast to the shore after beaching. The
i day was spent in removing stores, the
| transport lying very nearly bow un
der, but well afloat in the afterpart.
Later In the afternoon, without warn
ing, she slid off the steep beach and
i sunk in about six fathoms. A more
favorable or picturesque spot for a
' shipwreck could scarcely be found. The
island of Inoshima is about a dozen
miles from Hiroshima, the chief city of
| the inland sea. Almost instantly came
â  aid from the Japanese authorities, the
â  cruiser Yosohome being at once dis
] patched and the Red Cross Society
1 sending a relief expedition. The troops
; are loud in their appreciation of Japan
i ese kindness.
A pleasant episode for the shlp-
I wrecked men was the passing of the
| scene by the Empress of India. She
stopped for some hours and proffered
everything which could foe done in the
way of aid.
The damage by the recent typhoon
which swept the whole southern portion
: of Japan is even greater than at first
! reported. The appalling subsidence of
; the Bosshi mine in the island of Shiko
!ku entailed the loss of 600 lives. From
s<>me parts of the south reports still
| come in of hundreds of houses de
i stroyed, while every prefecture counts
jby hundreds its dead. An interesting
: incident in the Besshi mine catastrophe
was the final saving of five miners who
had been imprisoned in the earth for
Fix days by the caving In of the en
trance. ____^

FILIPINOS
MAY RELEASE
PRISONERS
Ask for Permission to Hold
a Conference With Gen
eral Otis.
SDeclal Dispatch to The Coll.
WASHINGTON, Sept. 19.â Secretary
Root to-day received a cablegram from
General Otis that seemed to indicate a
weakening on the part of the insur
gents In Luzon. It contained a proffer
to deliver the American prisoners who
have been for bo many months in the
hands of the insurgents, and also
sought permission to parley with Gen
eral Otis.
This cablegram was at once taken by
Secretary Root over to the Cabinet
meeting and formed the main topic for
discussion at the beginning of the Cab
inet session. However, upon reading ,
the message carefully its apparent ira
' portance seemed to diminish.
Secretary Root himself did not regard
the matter as of Importance at this
j'time. He recalled frequent efforts on
the part of the insurgents In the past
to gain time at critical moments by
! opening negotiations, ostensibly with
i the object of making $eace, and +ie was J
! not sure but this last offer wate some
thing of the same kind. However, the
! Secretary was of the opinion that it \
â  would fail to afford the insurgents any
I advantage. General Otis would receive
' any messenger and listen to him and
; make answer to his proposals, but this j
; would not restrain the military opera- i
tions in the slightest degree, and the
American arms would lose no ground,
no matter how the negotiations turned I
' out. There is a possibility also that the
! Insurgents may have become disheart-
I ened at the renewed and intense actlv
| ity in the direction of reinforcing Gen
i eral Otis and concluded, in view of the I
j great force gathering to open the cam- |
paign in the dry season, that further
! resistance would be useless.
It is pointed out as a noticeable fact
I that Aguinaldo's name does not appear
1 in General Otis" dispatch, and though
I this may not be of great significance.
i still among some of the army officials
! it Is surmised that there are serious
i dissensions among the insurgents and
I that, perhaps, some of the discordant
L elements are acting on their own re
sponsibility. The text of the dispatch
is as follows:
MANILA Sept. 19.â Adjutant General,
Washington: General Mac-Arthur reports ,
from Angeles the visit of two insurgent ;
. officers with a rr;iu<-st for permission to
send Into our lines American prisoners
mill :â¢' send to Manila a prominent Insur
| gent general officer for a conference. The
requested Interview was granted and the j
: Insurgent officers at Angeles return north j
this morning with Information. OTIS.
The capture of Lieutenant Gilrnore j
and his party was one of the most stir
ring incidents of the insurrection. It
occurred while the cruiser Yorktown
j was making a survey of the western
j coast of Luzon with a view to locating
some of the detached Spanish garrisons
which had long been cut off from the
populous centers. It was known that
a Spanish garrison had been SUITOUnd
! t-d at Baler, on the eastern coast, and
Lieutenant Gilmore, with a boat's party
from the Yorktown, was sent ashore to
reconnoiter the surrounding cotrntry.
He divided his command, one party re
maining near the shore, while another
pushed inland. This was the last heard
of the lieutenant and the fifteen men
who accompanied him. The other mem-
J bers of the party waited for a long
time, but got no trace of their compan
ions, and finally were obliged to return
to the ship without them.
Admiral Dewey at once reported the
loss to the Navy Department and be
fore he left Manila used every possi
ble means to learn the condition of the
captive Americans. At first it was
feared they had lost their Jives, but
later definite information was received
that the following were alive at San
Isidro, an insurgent stronghold:
Lieutenant Gilmore, Chief quarter
master Walton. Sailmakers" Mate Paul
Vandoit, Coxswain John Elsworth, Ap
prentice Peterson (third class). Lands- |
man Silvio Hrisolez, Landsman Lvman
Paul Edwards and Landsman Fred An
derson.
Captain Charles M. Rockefeller of the
Nineteenth Infantry is also supposed
to be a prisoner with the insurgents.
He disappeared last April and no trace
of him has been discovered.
REORGANIZING THE
GUARD AT PASADENA
PASADENA. Sopt. 19.â Company I.
Seventh Regiment, National Guard of
Callforniu. reorganized this evening by
oloctiiiK vow officera :ni<i choosing for
captain Captain D. M. Greene, v veteran
of three wars :m<l pernaps the oldest man
in the National Guard service under the
staff.
Captain Greene has seen sixteen years
of actual service. He served in the war
of the rebellion in the riixth Regiment,
California Volunteers, and the late llum
boldt County Indian war. He was twice
mentioned for gallant service. He enlisted
in the Spanish war and was captain of a
troop in the Sixteenth I'nited States Cav
alry. He is f>2 years of age.
Three members of the company were
removed for the good of the company.
They attempted to break up the company
by blackballing applicants for member
ship.

RECEPTION AT SAN JOSE
TO THE VOLUNTEERS
SAN JOSE, Sept. 19.â The local Red
Cross Society is preparing to give a warm
reception to the 250 volunteers who will
come here Thursday. These are some of
the men who have returned from the Phil
ippines and are now -suiting at the Pre
sidio to be mustered out.
They will be served a bountiful lunch
in St. James Park and given a rfde about
the town and valley. Nothing will be left
undone to give them a day of pleasure.
ENGINEER JONES DIES
OF HIS INJURIES
Was Badly Scalded by Escaping
Steam in the Wreck at
Capitola.
SANTA CRUZ. Sept. 19.-A. P. Jones,
the engineer who was so frig-htfully
scalded by escaping steam in the collision
on the Southern Pacific near Capitola on
Tuesday last, died at half-past 6 last
evening at his home at Pajaro.
Jones had been a trusted employe of
the Southern Pacific for many years and
was very popular on the road.
WHITE MAN'S
BURDEN IN THE
PHILIPPINES
The Unitarian Club
Discusses It.
THE Duties of Civilized Nations To
ward the Uncivilized was the sub
ject for discussion last night at the
annual meeting of the Unitarian
Club of California. It was dis-
cussed at the Merchants' Club in
the Mutual Life building after one of the
most enjoyable dinners ever given by the
organization. Fully 200 members and
guests sat at table and enjoyed the dis
cussion that followed. President Sheldon
G. Kellogg presided, and arranged near
him were the gentlemen underlined for
addresses on the subject. Hon. John Bar
rett, ex-Minister to Siam. was at his left,
and Professor Thomas Bacon of the Uni
versity of California at his right. Many
other distinguished guests were present.
In announcing The subject for discussion
President Kellogg said it might properly
be called "the white man's burden," and
he called upon Professor Bacon to lend
the first hand to pass it along.
Professor Bacon opened with the state
ment that the question was by no means
a new one. Many solutions had been of
fered in times cone by, and now a new
solution was offernble. The problem first
arose, he said, when the first discoveries
of the lifteenth and sixteenth centuries
laid open to the east coast of Europe a
multitude of men, alien in race and color,
for whom no provision had been made in
the medieval system of European thought.
Rudely and crudely the Europeans of
those times set about solving the ques
tion what to do with them.
"The first answer," continued Professor
Bacon, "was direct and easy. It seems a
hard answer to us. It seems one which
could only be suggested by the cruelest
instincts of man, but we misjudge those
persons, who had to solve that problem
and solve it suddenly, if we suppose
that there was really in their heart no
gentleness, no consideration, no human
Instinct. Their theory of how the civil
ized races of the world ought to treat the
uncivilized racesâ how the white man
ought to treat the man that was not
white â was very simple; the white man
ought to enslave the black man. Every
consideration of interest and religion
pointed to this solution of the problem.
What arrangement corfld be better than
that which should give money to the
white man in this world and eternal sal
vation to the black man in the next?
"Portugal and Spain had been- the ex
ponents of this first theory and England
and Holland of the other," continued the
speaker. "Their theory was that they
should go out and seize the outlying
parts of the earth, inhabited or uninhabi
ted, and use them for their own behoof.
"Very little religious zeal," said Professor
Bacon, "went into the upbuilding of the
British Empireâ very little religious zeal,
indeed. A few colonists on the east coast
of this continent were actuated by the
religious sense, and founded communities
for conscience sake; but mighty little re
ligion had that fine old pirate, Sir Fran
cis Drake, as he chased the galleons of
the Spanish king and captured them for
himself and his queen along the Spanish
main, or troubled the waters of the blue
Pacific with his heel."
What the English and Dutch adven
turers went for, said Professor Bacon,
was gain. It was on this principle, he as
serted, the British Empire in India was
built up.' He briefly traced the history
of English progress in the Far East, and
came again to the white man's burden.
He continued:
This new doctrine has grown quietly but
steadily. It has permeated the whole tf the
English people, but it has only come to our
notice lately when we became more inter
ested In those things that have found a
philosopher In Benjamin Kidd and a poet
in Itudyard KiplltiK. It is known to us as
the "White Man's Burden.'' Little signifi
cance It would have had for us it there was
not opening out a powerful sentiment
among the people who have most to do with

the settlement of the question of the duty
of civilize.! nations toward the uncivilized.
The theory of the white man's burden
may briefly be stated as this: It is the busi
ness and the interest and the duty of the
white manâ that Is, the man of civilization,
to rule, to care for, to uplift, to enlighten
and to bring. If possible, into the light of
modern civilization the other man wherever
he is found; that It Is at once their interest
and their duty to do this, with violence,
with tact, with kindness, as each may be
necessary; to do It whether the other man
likes it or not; to do it at Infinite and im
measurable cost alike to the white man
and the other man. and, if possible, to lift
this lower, alien, colored race to something
like the plane of civilization which the most
advanced nations have reached. In the
meantime it !b the business of the civilized
races of the worldâ the most Civilized races
of the earthâ to police the whole planet and
make people behave themselves Just as it is
the business of the best and most civilized
people of a community to police that wh >le
community so that the elements that are
disorderly and uncivilized shall be com
pelled to behave themselves. It is a part of
this theory that It Is the duty of the civil
ized nations to put an end to the nameless
horrors of heathen savagery and heathen
civilization wherever they exist.
Now. one word about the application of
thlfi doctrine of oursâ and it's a very sad
subject, but it is a subject we have got to
discurs fairly and freely, and In which
every voice (jas got to be heard and every
honest man must have a right to express
his opinion, howover unpopular. Mr. I".!
--win Atkinson, with whom I differ greatly,
has got Just as much right, and Is* just as far
removed from any treasonable purposr- or
treasonable work or Influence as any other
man In this country. If the beginning of
our Imperial career Is going to be the sup
pression, either by law or by public
clamor, of absolutely free discussion of
every subject which can Involve the safety
and welfare of this nation, I say thÂ» be
ginning of our imperial career is goinp to be
marked with a blnckneps that will blast
us altosether.
In regard to the Philippinesâ for. of
course, the Philippines is the subject of
Which we speakâ riow did we get them? I
don't know. I didn't want them. I think
most of the intelligent men of this country
didn't want them, and as for the rest 'if
us. ns Mr. Dnuley expresses It. they don't
know the difference between the Philippines
and canned goods. Anil certainly if the
American people had any idea that the an
nexation of the Philippines would have r-jst
us one-half of what it baa already cfct i
us they never would have consented to it
If they had been consulted. Some people j
think that the annexation of the Philip
pines was providential. Perhaps the Lor.l
did It: perhaps the devil did it: perhaps
MrKinley did it. I don't know but I hi?
peet that all three hnd a hand in It, but I
do know that it's done and It can't be un
done.
The white man's burden In the. Philip- I
pines, Professor Bacon concluded, was j
' the day's work rut out for the people of
the United States.
"We have grit to do It patiently," he
said. "We have got to do it with perfect
consciousness of what it costs ami with
really no hope of reward except that of
our own consciences. But we are going
to do it."
Hon. John Barrett, ex-Minister to Siam,
was introduced as soon as the applause
at Professor Bacon's effort lad ceased.
Mr. Barrett told of his oflicial trawls in
the far East, especially in the Philip
i pines, and gave as his deduction from his
I observations that with proper men, not
politicians to educate the Filipino, he
would become a valuable acquisition to
the nation. On the score of morality In
the far East he said was not so cer- I
tain that the Asiatic might have a white
man's burden.

NEW YORK, Sept. 19.â Kid McCoy
had little difficulty in petting away
with Steve O'Donnell in the arena of
the Broadway Athletic Club to-night.
Despite O'Drnnell's advantage of
height, reach and weight McCoy went
to him gradually without taking any
careless liberties and soon had his big
adversary in trouble.
O'Donnell never landed a good blow
on McCoy, while the KM was gaining
ground In each succeeding round. His
left was always getting to the face,
head or wind and O'Donnell was
puzzled. He staggered frequently, and
in the sixth round went down from a
left swing. He remained down as long
as the rules allowed, but it was all u\>
with the big fellow. McCoy went after
him with swinging lefts and rights and
coon floored him with a hard left swing,
from the effects of which he was unable
to recover until counted out.
Steve had to be assisted to his feet
by the referee, while McCoy walked to
his corner smiling and without a mark.
The betting opened at 3 to 1 on Me
Coy, but these odds were increased to
10 to 1, with very few takers.
Every inch of the vast seating space
In the clulihouse was occupied half an
hour after the doors were opened and
hundreds of people thronged the streets
in front of the building early, crowding
in for standing room. Since the club
house was opened th<re has never been
such a large attendance. The men were
introduced to go twenty rounds at
catchweights and both received a
hearty reception.
Round Iâ They sparred at long range for
some moments. Steve tried two lefts for
the body, hut failed to land. McCoy sent
two lefts to the face and landed a light
left swing to the iaw. Then he sent a
similar blow to the chin without getting
anything back from the big fellow.
O'Donnell was entirely on the defensive
and Mif. >v amused the crowd by giving
State three hard backhand slaps on the

i.o chanced and held O'Donnell so safe

Delightful Social Function Given on
Its Anniversary.
There were many Native Sons and Na
tive Daughters last night in the beauti
fully decorated hall of the Native Sons'
building, on the occasion of the anniver
sary ball given by Orinda Parlor of tho
Native Daughters. This function, like
many that have preceded it, was most
enjoyable for the members of this par
lor are always up to the times in social
events, presenting on occasions like the
one last evening the newest dances. The
grand march was a very pretty sight and
was Joined by a great many couples.

BILLY REED COACHES
A TEAM AT STANFORD
Babe Burnett Will Be Beady for
Work in a Very Few
Days.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Sept. IS.â
Billy Reed, fullbark of Harvard's victor
ious team, appeared on tlv- football field to
night and took his position behind the line
for the edification of Stanford's gridiron
candidates. He went through the usual
practice movements with the team and
gave them the benefit of some of h!.Â« Har
vard experience. A? Reed left the field he
heartllv shook the hand of Chamberlin,
the man who gave him so much trouble
on th.' Vale line last Thanksgiving day.
Reed, who is 'me of America's foremost
ftthletes and captains, leaves his Relmont
home to-morrow for Harvard, where he
will *fo into active training for this fall's
campaign.
Treasurer Leiter to-night received word
from Babe Burnett that he would be on
the campus Friday, ready for work. This
means the stiffening of Stanford's center
trio, and may prove to be the deciding
feature of the game.
INSURANCE MEN WILL
VISIT THE OLYMPIANS
Athletic Club Officers to Arrange a
Special Programme of Boxing
and Wrestling.
The first of a series of entertainments
in the, gymnasium of the Olympic CU..J,
under the new directorate, will be on the
evening of October 4. It will be insur
ance men's night, all persons employed
in Insurance offices being the special
guests of the club.
There will be boxing and wrestling by
the cleverest amateurs. The Rev. W. H.
L. O'Rourke, chaplain of the Seamen's
Institute and a believer in muscular
Christianity, will have a score of sailor
lads on hand to show how adept they
are with the gloves.
The junior class has been re-estab
lished, the aim being to build up the gym
nasium classes and the field athletes. A
large number of Lowell High School
boys have already signified their inten
tion of joining. "William Smyth, who has
made a success of this work, has been
engaged as Instructor. The limit of age
Is from 17 to 21 years, and no initiation fee
is exacted, merely monthly dues.
RUBY RAYMOND IS
RELEASED ON BONDS
Trial of the San Jose Woman Who
Harbored Young Girls.
Ruby Raymond, the young woman who
Is charged with entking Cora Danforth
and Daisy Miller from homo for Improper
purposes, wns on trial before Judge Mo
gan laÃt night for her offense.
After evidence had been introduced, the
court took the case under advisement.
The defendant's bonds were approved
and she was allowed her liberty.
When the case came up for trial last
week. Ruby failed to put In an appear
ance and Judge Mogan forfeited her
bonds and issued a bench warrant for
her arrest. The testimony given at the
trial last night showed conclusively that
the girls were under age.

OUTLOOK IS
BRIGHT FOR
FINE RACING
Horses, Jockeys and Bookies
in Plenty for the Open
ing on Saturday.
The California racing season of IR!>9 and
extending- well into the year 1900 will en
joy a renewal at Oakland track on Satur
day, September 23. The crack jockeys of
the country will not be seen in the pigskin
on the opening day, nor will all of the best
handicap horses of the day face the bar
-1 Her. But by the foregoing statement it
must not be construed that there will be
no competent pilots or cood horses to
place wagers on. Every incoming train
from the East brings a fresh supply of
equine talent, the very latest to date be
ing the stable of P. Ryan, one of the most
consistent that ever ran here. Mr. Ryan's
string arrived at Oakland on Sunday, and
comprises the following very useful per
formers: Synia, Summer, Jerid, Marcato,
Silver Tone, Cyril, Afamada. Kacivah and
a yearling by Fonso. Besides there will
be represented locally Burns & Water
house with an immense stable of bread
winners, including their two St. Louis
purchases, Bit of Fashion and Southern
Girl, J. G. Brown, out of IJbertlne, Bam
boulia, Monrovia, Diomed and a dozen
others; the lighter strings of "Curley"
Shields, McMahon & Gerhardy, John
Humphrey, Tom Boyle, Caesar Young,
Dun Honig, S. F. Capps, McHugh & Lin
nell, "Hanger" Jones, Dagjjie Smith and
a host of others.
The jockeys, for the greater part, are
not above mediocrity, including among
them Quill Morse, Eddie Joins,
Macklin, Frank Lmffy Tommy But
ler, Johnny Sullivan and Ruiz, but
they are all capable of doing good
work in the saddle. Buchanan is an
extremely promising lad, and if Atkins &
Lottridge can persuade "Monk" Coburn
to wear an ice cap every other day he
too can win an occasional race. Of course,
Charley Thorpe, the Western favorite,
will be on hand, and praise in this in
stance Is unnecessary. His past record
speaks for itself.
To start off with the bookmaking brig
ade will be limited, though at present it
looks as if the ensemble would be some
thing like John Humphrey, Schwartz &
Co., Harlan & Co., Hoffman te Co., Paul
Ridley, Phil Archibald, Otis Evans and
Johnny Lyons. The latter was here last
winter, but did not book. He has a bank
roll large enough to derail a Southern Pa
cific train and will give the chalk and
chamois a hard "go. The firms men
tioned are almost certainties, and there
will perhaps be a dozen or more In all lay
ing prices.
An evening paper which has been decry
ing the wicked horrors of a racetrack
war with all the attendant atrocities of
the naughty ungovernable turf advisers
recently printed the following significant
paragraph: "Mr. Corrigan finished bis
Beason at Chicago Saturday. He will be
here directly and then there will be war
in earnest." If t lie writer had said "the
Beason finished Mr. Corrigan at Saratoga
and he had retired to Hawthorne, in
which track he owns |40,000 worth of
stock, to prepare for another sortie," then
he would have hit the nail on the head.
Corrigan will probably hold a grand pow
wow of all the medicine men at Haw
thorne Lodge, signal tires for miles around
will illuminate the heavens, weird ghost
dances will end the wild orgies and we
may expect to see the grand old chief of
Cicero Township appeur here bedaubed
â¢with war paint and bedecked with feath
ers as we never paw him before.
Golden Rule, greatest of the bad lot of
two-year-olds racing at Ingleside and
Oakland last spring, a cheap plater at
Saratoga, has finally found his levfl. He
can win at Hawthorne, make Time turn
a ghastly hue, and now his friends again
say he Is a great colt. Even Corngan
found a Chicago reporter the other day
who believed it and printed such stuff in
the paper ho represented, imn't those peo
ple know that a good selling plater can
run rings around a trashy bunch of plat
ers and almost hang up a record? But
put this same horpp in with some handi
cap horses-, and his name is utterly for
got at the finish. The pnor fellow is car
ried so fast the first part of the journey
that he "cracks" and asks the others to
'.'break the news to mother."

ors the intricacies of the percentage sys

SHAMROCK'S GAFF BREAKS DURING A TRIAL SPIN.
THK tuning-up of the yacht Shamrock, challenger for the America cup,
preparatory to the first of the series of races on October 3, has given
Sir Thomas Upton and his associates some anxious moments. The most
serious accident tending to delay the preparation of the yacht occurred
last Wednesday. The metal pan! which supports the mainsail collapsed when
there was comparatively little pressure on it. It was not the extent of the
accident, but the possibilities it opened up that gave the visiting yachts
men their shock. If this metal spar lie so weak as to bend like a piece of
lead pipe, then all the spars are defective and may collapse at a critical mo
ment. The broken gaff was at once replaced by one of those kept In reserve.
William Fife. th._- designer, 1p said to have recommended the metal spars
against the advice of Thornycroft. one of the most celebrated of England's
naval architects.
SHAMROCK
DID NOT MAKE
FAST TIME
Experts, After the Trial Spin,
Say the Columbia Will
Win the Race.
Fpeclal Dispatch to The CSa.ll.
NEW YORK. Sept. 19.â Winds of vary
ing strength marked to-day's trial spin
of the Shamrock. From seven knots an
hour at first It blew fully twelve knots
toward the finish. Whenever a strong
puff struck the challenger she moved off,
full of life. Taking the day as It turned
out, experts were unanimous in saying
that the Defender, If she had been with
the green cutter, would have passed
through the ordeal satisfactorily, and
that the Columbia, had she been there,
would have been "bellwether" from start
to finish.
Sir Thomas Upton and his associates
decided to send the yacht to windward
on the first leg of the course selected, but
the shifting of the wind shortly after the
start prevented that. The second and
final less of reaching, however, were all
that they could have desired.
As the wind was south by east, the tug
Lawrence was sent ten miles from the
Scotland lightship, but the breeze backed
to the south, so that the weather mark
was made in three boards, the second be
ing of long duration. The second leg was
to Bandy Hook lightship, about eleven
nautical miles, and the third was back to
the Scotland lightship, four and an eighth
nautical miles. The second leg was a
broad reach, and the last also, with the
wind abaft the beam. It required 1 hour
38 minutes and 20 seconds to cover the
first leg. 1 hour 32 minutes and 40 seconds
for the second leg. and 23 minutes for the
last. This was by outside timing. By the
official timer on the Erin the legs "were
made in this wise:
First, 1 hour 37 minutes 33 seconds;
second, 1 hour S3 minutes 37 seconds;
third, 23 minutes 18 seconds.
The whole distance of twenty-five and
an eighth miles was sailed In 3 hours 4
minutes and 15 seconds, unofficial timing,
or 3 hours 4 minutes and 28 seconds by
the Erin's figures.
The crews of the Shamrock were at
times shifted from abaft the mainmast
to the stern and back again. Mr. Fife or
Skipper Hogarth trying experiments with
their weight. The boat, however, made
slow progress, as in 32 minutes from the
time of starting she had gone only about
three and a half nautical miles.
WANTED TO KILL A
MURDERER'S FRIEND
CHINESE WITH A BIG GTJN IN
JAIL.
Ah Fong, Who Was Shot at by Wong
Hing, the Captured Criminal,
Looks for Trouble.
Wong Hlng, the San Jose murderer, who
was captured at Fresno last Sunday, will
be brought to this city by Officer Rey
nolds to-day. Yesterday Ah Fong
was made a target for the captured man's
bullets on September 13 on Spofford alley
In this city, was arrested by Sergeant
Duke and posse while he was looking for
one of Hing's friends with a 44-caliber re
volver, which he concealed under his
coat. His name was entered on the small
book at the Central police station and ho
will be held as a witness in the trial of
Wong Hing before any charge is placed
against him.
The arrest yesterday was only one of a
series of events which have hat'i
since a slave girl was spirited from a
house on Jackson street and taken to
San Jose. Some trouble arose over her
possession, and L<ee OngPoon was mur
dered and Deputy Sheriff Mackey of San
Jose shot and in"jTirecl by Wong Hing.
On September 13 the deputy sheriff whr*
was shot brought two Chinamen. Ah,
Fong and Ah Yum. to this city to assist
him in capturing the criminal. While th<i
two Chinamen were passing along Spof
ford alley on that <lay they were sighted,
by Hing, who fired three shots at Ah
Fong and again escaped. One of the
leaden missiles passed through the trous
ers of Ah Yum and narrowly missed kill
ing a Chinese laborer in a store opposite.
Tling was taken after a desperate strug
gle in Fresno on Sunday.
Yesterday afternoon Sergeant Duke dis-.
oovered Ah Fong passing along Waverli â¢y
place, and he noted that the Chlaaman'a
actions were suspicious. He at once took,
him into custody, and whon he searched
him found a large 44-caliber revolver on
bis person. He had his hand on the
weapon when ho was taken, and It was
his Intention to shoot one of Hing's
friends on sight. The pnliee say that the
trio of Chinamen implicated in thr shoor
ing are all wicked men. and do not think
that the trouble will end with yesterday's
arrest. The affair has been in'the nature
of a highbinder's war and has caused
much commotion in the Chinese quarters

COLONEL MILEY
DIES AT MANILA
THE sad news nf the death of Colonel John D. Miley, inspector general
of volunteers, in Manila was received in this city yesterday by General
Shafter. whom he formerly served as aid-de-camp. His death occasioned
genuine sorrow among his former comrades in this city, and many ex
pressions of repret were heard at General Shafter's headquarters at the loss
of a thorough gentl< man and a brave soldier.
Colonel Miley leaves a widow and four children, who are at present living
with her father,' Colonel Mordecai, at Benlcia Barracks. His brother married
a daughter of A. B. Butler of Fresno. Many messages of condolence have
been forwarded to the bereaved family.
lieutenant Colonel Miley was ,a native of Illinois, and was graduated
from West Point in 1887. In 1890 he came to this city with the Fifth Artillery.
During his â  - at the Presidio few officers were as popular with the
rank and file as he. and a host of friends attest his popularity.
When war broke oul with Spain, as Major Miley he went to Cuba as an
aid on General Shafter's staff, and he. together with Captain W. H. McKit
trick and Lieutenant J. S. Wheeler, raised the American flag at Santiago. His
conduct in that campaign won for him promotion to a lieutenant colonelcy.
He was a conspicuous figure on the firing line and was at all times fearless
in danger.
Last February <"nlonel Miley was ordered to the Philippines and was ap
pointed inspector general of volunteers nn General Otis' staff. He served In
that capacity until he was made Collector of Customs at Manila and tfivon
sup< rvision of the entire customs service in the Philippines. It was on the
6th inst. that the dread disease attacked him. and death yesterday cut short
a promising career. Colonel Miley's fank in the regular army was first lieu
tenant of the Second Artillery.
When the intelligence reached the city that Colonel Miley was prostrated
by fever General Shafter wired General (His. requesting a daily cable mes
sage, reporting the condition of the patient: and every day thereafter a
message was wired to headquarters of the department. About a. week ago
the physician's diagnosis of the case was cabled from Manila, and medical
men here were then convinced that the patient could not recover. Mrs.
Miley, a soldier? daughter and a soldier's wife, bore up bravely under the
shock caused by the cablegram announcing her husband's death.
If Colonel Miley's life had been spared he would have acquired high
rank and great distinction in the army. He was a soldier of many ac
complishments, dauntless on the field of battle and progressive In the ad
ministration of military affairs in time of peace. His capacity for work
was marvelous and his industry remarkable. Generals Shafter and Gra
ham had the utmost confidence in his judgment.
Colonel Miley was familiar with the high power guns and the dynamite
battery defending the harbor. He gained by study and daily observation a
practical knowledge of the method employed in handling dynamite projec
tiles and applying compressed air.
Before he left San Francisco to engage in the Cuban campaign he ma
terially increased The insurance on his life for the benefit of his family.
He was an affectionate, home loving man.Jiut when the call of duty came
he was ready and resolute.

Roy Johnston, the recruit who poured
out his woes in a letter to the President,
has been acquitted by a court-martial for
his offense. He wrote to the President,
asking f<r his discharge, and criticizing
his officers very severely. It was shown
at the court-martial that at the time he
wrote the letter he did not know an of
ficer when he saw one in uniform, and
as he begged to be let off he was ac
quitted. Ii Is an offense against army
regulations for a private to address the
President without sending his letter
through his commanding officer. This
was not considered so much of an of
fense in the case of Johnston as what
he wrote. He denounced his officers and
cast slurs upon his comrades, and so the
matter was taken up. .
For sleeping on post Private Doll T.
Long of the Thirty-first Infantry has
been sentenced to seven days' confine
ment and to forfeit $11 of his pay.
Burt Snelson. who enlisted under the
name of Edward Robinson, a discharged
soldier died at the general hospital yes
terday. He came to this country from
Manila already discharged, and it is not
known what command he belonged to.
He died of consumption.

IS NOW A CAPTAIN
IN SIGNAL CORPS
CAPTAIN CHARLES H. GORDON.
ANNOUNCEMENT was made in the
telegraphic dispatches from Wash
ington D. <"., last Monday that First
Lieutenant Charles 11. Gordon has
been promoted to the rank of captain.
The officer who has been thus honored
is now on his way to this city on the
transport City of Para which will arrive
shortly. Ills promotion Is the direct
result of gallant service with the Signal
i 'Mips, of which ho was a member, during
several fierce engagements.
Lieutenant Gordon left this city on the
port City of Rio do Janeiro with the
Eighteenth Company, I'nited States sig
nal Corps, and on arriving In the Philip
pines was placed in charge of the Tondo
district On the night that Manila was
taken, Lieutenant Gordon was assigned
to General Mac Arthur's division as signal
r.ftlcer. He was the first to establish
:ommunication with the American fleet
in that eventful night.
He continued to serve with General
MaeArthur during the latter's advance
!nto Caloocan and the Interior. He wa.s
me of five men who were mentioned by
:heir commanding officer for conspicuous
:ind distinguished services during those
trying times. He was subsequently as
signed to General Lawton's command
md though not a member of General H
;. Otis' brigade, was highly regarded by
:hat officer, whose acquaintance he made
jn the trip to the Philippines.
The new captain was born In Maryland
md was educated in Poughkeepsie, N. Y.
t-Ie is the son of Colonel D. S. Gordon
J. S. A., retired, and a brother of Philip
3ordon. ticket agent of the Southern
Pacific Company in this city. Captain
3ordon Is 36 years of age and was at one
ime agent of the Southern Pacific at
Draey. He subsequently resigned to go
nto the mining business, which he left
o enlist in the service of his country.
A'hen he arrives here with his company
t will be for the purpose of being
nustered out, and he will then join his
anjily at the residence, 1707 Scott street.
STRIKE ON THE
TRANSPORTS IS
STILL GOING ON
Boilermakers Will Not
Give In.
Everything is in statu quo on the Gov
ernment wharf. There is no work being
done by the boiler makers on the Sher
man, and there may be trouble to-day
when a call for men to work on the Gle
nogle is made. There was only a few
hours' work required on the Tacoma to
get her ready for sea and this was done
by the men. bo the Tacoma and George
W. Elder will sail for Manila to-day.
They will take a regiment between them,
and Captain Barneson expects to get
them away by 4 p. m.
Tbe boiler makers Intend to stick out
for their demands and the contractors
have decided not to yield. In the event
of the Government being unable to get
the repairs made here it may send the
transports 10 Manila under a reduced
steam pressure and then have the ves
sels overhauled at Hongkong on the way
back to San Francisco. Should Uncle
Sam be forced to take that course, then
all the repairs to the transports wouid
be done at the same lime and there would
be a loss of at least $10,tX>0 a week in
wages alone to rian Francisco.
The painters made no move yesterday.
Some of them are gelling $;â ! 50, some t'i
and the majority iZ 50 for an eight-hour
day. For overtime they get double pay.
Thus a painter goes to work at S a. m.
and lays oIT at noun for lunch. Begin
ning again at 1 p. m., he works to 5 p. m.
and lays off an hour for supper. Then
from ti to 10 p. m. he works overtime
and for the twelve hours' work gets two
days' pay. The men are to have, a con
ference with Captain Bennett to-day, but
he does not anticipate any trouble. The
payroll of the painters <>n the transports
last week was over $6000.
There are live large steamers on the

N S. W., with a cargo of coal, and the

expected to leave the same port any day

In Chinese waters and will come here
via Nagasaki. The Westminster is Zolu
tuns net, the Anglo Australian 2561 tons
net the Port Stephens 127* tons net, the
Pak Ling &575 tons net and the Moyune
aulo tons net burden. Each or them win
merefore easily carry a regiment of soi
aiers and all will make splendid trans
ports. With these vessels aaued to the
fleet aiready engaged Uncle Sam will
nave no diiuculty in rustling a big body
of men to the Piiillpinet,, and it now be
gins to look as thougn the reoellion wouid
oe put down In the time specified by the
administration.

INCREASE OF CRIME
AMONG SOLDIERS
GENERAL SHAFTER ASKED TO
PROVIDE A PROVOST GUARD.
The numerous oases of soldiers called in
the Police Courts recently charged with
various crimes have called forth a re
monstrance from the Judges, and yester
day Judge Graham sent the following
letter to General Shafter:
As one of the Judges of the Police
Court of this city and county, the
numerous depredations committed in
this city by the recruits have been
brought forcibly to my notice. Nearly
f-very morning m^n in uniform are
brought into the Police Courts charged
with all crimes from drunkenness to
murder. I notice that nearly all the
crinu-s committed by the soldiers are
committed while they are under the
influence of liquor and late at night.
It has been suggested to me by officers
that if a provost guard were estab
lished downtown to "round up" and
take charge of obstreperous, vicious or
intoxicated soldiers it would result in
advantage both to the citizens and to
the soldiers themselves.
Yesterday William Brown of the Thirty
sixth Infantry appeared before Judge
Graham to answer a charge of begging.
He was arrested on Sixteenth street, be
tween Mission and Howard, on Monday
night while soliciting alms from pedes
trians. Lieutenant Peck of his company
told the Judge that the regiment was to
leave by the transport Grant for Manila
in about ten days, and the- Judge ordered

TABLES TURNED
ON A PORTLAND
SHIP'S RUNNER
Was Carried to Sea
Against His Will.
The British ship Muskoka. which sailed
for Europe from Astoria a couple of days
ago, had considerable trouble in securing
a crew. The boarding masters on the
Columbia River wanted $80 a man, so the
captain employed Frank Turk and J.
Lynch to get him twenty-two sailors.
Turk came to San Francisco and secured
sixteen men whom he shipped north on
the steamer Columbia. He could get no
more sailors here, so he wired Lynch to
go to Puget Sound and pick up six A B's
there. Lynch went to Seattle and there
found the American ship Rufus E. Wood,
ready to sail for Sydney, N. S. W. He
went aboard and tried to get six of the
crew to desert, but while he was
persuading the men the tug came along
side and took the ship to sea. Lynch is
now on his way to Australia, and the
captain of the Wood may be depended
upon to see that he works his passage,
tailing to get any more men the Muskoka
put to sea short handed.
The Mail steamer Colon arrived from
Panama and way ports yesterday with
fifteen cabin passengers and eighteen
Chinese in transit for Hongkong. Among
her cargo was over $iuo ,<JUU worth of
ir>asure. in Central American silver
there was J15.554; three bars of gold and
silver were valued at JZS.iNiO, fifty-live bars
of silver at $42 .uw. thirty-six sacks of
precipitate of silver at $20,uu0 and one
case of gold dust on which no value was
placed.
The steiimer America Maru was more
damaged Sy the lire that broke out
in her coal bunkers than was at first an
ticipated. While in Yokohama the coal
caught tire, but was put out, and on
September lv she sailed for San Fran
cisco via Honolulu. A few days later she
put back, the coal in her hunkers having

that the fire had been put out, but the

damaged." It will now probably be the
first week in October before the America
Maru gets here.
P. Kilcoyne, one of the Market-street
Railroad Company's employes, was badly
injured on Kearny street yesterday. He
was helping to repair the railroad track
and got crushed betwee two railroad ties.
His right leg was badly lacerated and
his lelt leg broken. He was treated at
the Harbor Receiving Hospital by Dr.
Robinson and was afterward removed to
the railroad hospital.
Xels Hanson, a sailor on the ship Ori
ental, fell into the hold of the vessel
yesterday. At the Harbor Hospital Dr.
Robinson found that both his wrists were
sprained and his face badly lacerated.
Five stitches had to be taken in his eye
brow before he could leave the operating
table.
Andrew Olsen had a row with his land
lord at 43 Steuart street last Monday night
and yesterday had a lacerated scalp
sewed up at the Harbor Hospital. An
drew asked the landlord for a candle with

he says.
Captain Thompson Colly has turned
marine artist since leaving the sea for a
life ashore, and some of his efforts in the
painter's art are very good. He has made
a particularly good picture of the San
Francisco owned ship Sintram, which he
is going to send to his friend. Captain
Matthews, in New Zealand. Painting is
now Captain Colly's hobby, and although
he has taken it up late in life he is raak
ing rapid progress.

Twenty-sixth Infantry, was before Judge
Mogan on a charge of disturbing the
peace. He and three other soldiers were
creating a disturbance at Market and
Fifth streets late Monday night by fight
ing among themselves and using the most
profane and vulgar language. Policeman
Tillman cautioned them to keep quiet,
but they would not, and Harris got a
brick and threw it at the officer. Till
man placed him under aVrest, but he
fniight so hard that the officer had to use
his club to bring him under subjection.
The Judge convicted Harris yesterday
morning and will sentence him to-day.
"Complaints have recently been made
to me, said the Judge, "about men in
uniform insulting ladles on the streets,
and it. is time that something was done to
put a stop to such conduct. The com
plaints have beer, about men who are con
nected with the casual detachments, who
seem to be undor the impression that they
can get drunk and run the city because
they wear a uniform. Men who have seen
Bervice are imbued with more gentlemanly
instincts and will not insult women, and
the first one of these casuals brought be
fore me on such a charge will be made an
example of."
Harris claims that he is an old soldier,
having seen twelve years' service, and
he and his three friends were only having
n good time when the officer arrested
him.
