THE TAX LEVY.
IT is vhetiier the annals ot san rranciscu
furnish a more appalling instance of dirt.v politics
than was supplied by the morning
: Democracy yesterday. In attempt
itical capital out of the re-arranged
tax levy, passed by the Supervisors on Monday, that
sheet descended to a level of moral degradation com
pared with which the an- of the ordinary ward poli
lity of exalted statesmanship.
Fherchave been in this city many conspicuous ex
of political wallowing in the pursuit of office,
which might be cited numerous assaults upon
private character designed to break down the aspira
if worthy men willing to serve the public; but
the oldest inhabitant, we venture to say, will fail to
recall an instance where a new-paper, for the purpose
of securing a petty political advantage of doubtful
value, lias deliberately misrepresented the facts of a
ux levy and with devilish malignity falsified its fig
ures. Nor is there in our whole history a case in
which there has been so successful an imitation oi
Satan's methods. We doubt whether the devil, mas
i the livery of heaven, would have been
equal to the task of enacting such a part in the in
terest of "f rnmeni "
In order to demonstrate the contemptible character
of the Examiner's assault upon the Supervisors in
connection with the tax levy it is only necessary to
appeal to the aci '>n Monday morning that sheet
printed ti in which it quoted the changes mi
the provisional levy suggested by Mayor Phelan.
The appropriations agreed upon in this levy by the
Supervisors "ii August 7 amou $2,520.18(3.
Mayor Phelan made changes last week, footing up
;, among which were extra allow*
$71,080 for the School Depart
ment, $31,296 !<>r the Street Department,
and $25,000 for the Fire Department. In order to
bring the levy within the pledged rate of $1 upon a
total valuation of $.^50,000,000 the Mayor placed
amounts agcregatins $-Ml.too â required for defi
ciencies, judgments, election expenses and the inter
est and funds â outside the pledge, thus rec
ognizing and indorsing the principle that expendi
irres not current might be met in that way.
Not only did the Examiner interview the Mayor
and approve I 1 '- proposed changes, but it pointed
proudly to the fact that the rate had been fixed at
0Q.5 cents by that official. The Supervisors were, in
the same article, dared to alter the figures, although
between the lines there was expressed no uncertain
hope that they .vould do so, and thereby manufacture
political capital for the Democratic party.
But the Supervisors did not alter the figures. On
the contrary, they adopted them bodily. To the list
of exemption-, however, they added $i_>s,ooo for the
acquisition for park purposes of the lands bounded
by Dupont, California and Pine street- and St. Mary's
place. To this appropriation every man of them had
been pledged, including the two Democrats and the
two RÂ«publicans who voted against it. This money
i> not necessary for current expenses. It comes
fairly within the principle ixcmption set up
by Mayor Phelan and indorsed In the F.xamincr in
the case of deficiencies, judgments, election expenses
i<nd interest and sinking funds. If the Mayor was
authorized to construe his pledge in favor of those
exemptions, why were not the Supervisors authorized
to construe theirs in behalf of a piedge which they
had made in good faith and which, they were honestly
bound to keep?
The tax levy is 7.7 cents in excess of the pledge cf
$1 upon a total valuation of $.^50,000,000. but all save
two and a fraction cents of that excess was not only
suggested and requested by Mayor Phelan, but it was
1 by the Democratic minority in the Board
of Supervisors aid ratified by the Examiner on Mon
(!ay>Â»Tl' two and a fraction cents wns
levied in pursuance of a pledge given to Rev. Father
Otis iii behalf of St. Mary.-. Park by every Super
visor, including the Democrats. Was not this pledge,
which, was violated by the Democrats, as sacred as
â¦lie other? Who, in short, are guilty of the greater
moral turpitudeâ th( -c Supervi <>rs '.vho honorably
Kept their pledge by following Mayor Phelan's con
struction of the j.'e- !;_:<â . or those who, in order ;<>
make low, partisan politics, broke their plighted faith
to an earnest minister of the Gospel who is striving
to abolish b city plague spoi and elevate the mornls
of the people?
It is difficult 10 discuss with patience a newspaper
which, in the manufacture of partisan polities, thus
falsifies the notorious facts of local history. Per
haps the incident does not justify the feeling we dis
play over it. since the Examine* has' long been
abandoned morally and is beyond reformation. But
it present? us with an opportunity to say what every
fcir-minded man in San Francisco knows to be the
truth, namely, that the present Board of Supervisors
hnve served the public faithfully; that they have kept
all their pledges honestly, and that the record s! lllW c,
them in every respect to be devoted to good, eco
nomical, respectable, efficient and honest government.
Any newspaper which withholds the credit due to such
men â which misrepresents them for political gain
not only works for bad and corrupt government, but
convicts itself of insincerity and base motives.
Justice Rodden has now discovered that the habit
of being "too previous" is one that the public will not
tolerate in a judicial position, and that it is known to
the law by a name almost as ugly as the Justice him
self.
THE SAN FKANCTSCO CALL. WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 20, 1899.
RUSSIA'S CLOSED PORTS.
THE report comes from Copenhagen that
Czar Nicholas has signed a ukase closing for
ever the Russian ports on the Pacific Ocean and
the Baltic and Black seas to any but Russian ships.
to take effect as soon as the Middle Eurffpe canal and
trans-Siberian railway are completed, in 1901. This
: fixes Russian commercial policy as far as "the open
i door" is concerned. Nothing is said of its applica
tion to the ports which Russia will control in China,
but its application to those on the Russianized coast
of Manchuria is probably the reason for its issue.
I Those ports will be gateways to Chinese com
! merce, and Russian occupation is ostensibly in that
irterest.
Since Napoleon's Berlin decree no public act has
been of such interest to the commercial world as this
decree by Nicholas. It brushes all Russian commer
cial treaties aside, and is an act of universal abroga
tion of every such convention entered into between
i the empire and the nations.
To the United States it is of peculiar significance.
Our treaty with Russia, negotiated by James Bu
chanan, December 6, 1832. provides that: "There
shall be between the territories of the high contract
ing parties a reciprocal liberty of commerce and
navigation. The inhabitants of their respective states
shall, mutually, have liberty to enter the ports, places,
rivers and territory of each party, wherever foreign
j commerce is permitted. Russian vessels arriving,
either laden or in ballast, in all ports of the United
i States, and reciprocally vessels of the United States
1 arriving either laden or in ballast in the ports of the
! empire of Russia, shall be treated on their entrance,
during their stay and at their departure, upon the
same footing as national vessels coming from the
same place, with respect to the duties of tonnage. All
kinds of articles of commerce and merchandise which
may be lawfully imported into the ports of the empire
of Russia in Russian vessels may also be imported in
vessels of the United States, without paying other
or higher duties or charges, of whatever kind or de
nomination, levied in the name or to the profit of the
Government, local authorities or private establish
ments whatsoever, than if the said merchandise or
articles of commerce had been imported in Russian
vessels. And. reciprocally, all kinds of merchandise
and articles of commerce may enter the ports of the
United States in Russian vessels upon the same
terms: in each case whether the vessels clear from
ports of the country to which they belong, or from
the ports of any other foreign country. All articles
of commerce and merchandise which may lawfully
be exported from either country in its own vessels
may be exported therefrom in the vessels of either
country on equal terms."
It will l>c seen that the ukase is practically a de
cree of abrogation of every line of this treaty. We
are not aware of the terms of Russian treaties of com
merce and navigation with other nations than ours,
but of course they exist and are abrogated the same
ay ours.
No explanation is made public of the reasons of
policy which have dictated this extraordinary action.
A large commerce has sprung up between San
Francisco and Vladivostok and Russian Pacific ports.
Indeed, our total commerce with Russia has in
creased. In iBgB our trade with that empire, for the
fiscal year ending June .30. was $8,317,573. Of this
$618,015 was with the Russian ports in Asia, and
$252,426 of it went from San Francisco.
( >ur Russian trade for seven months preceding
June 30. iSqq, was $8,103,230. of which $795,006 was
with her Asiatic ports. This commerce is not segre
gated into United States customs districts, so that
San Francisco's -hare is included in the total. The
foreign commerce of this port for the same seven
month- was. in exports, $16,280,026. and in imports
$24,510,006, a total of $40,790,032. It is fair to as
sume, therefore, that we got our share of the increase
with Russian Asia.
In some of its aspects the ukase seems to be a
decree of non-commercial intercourse with the world."
If the other treaty nations retaliate by closing their
ports to Russian ships it is difficult to understand
how Russia could have any foreign commerce at all,
and her position would be exactly like that of China
and Japan before they were forcibly opened to for
eign trade. We have not at hand the statistics which
reveal the volume of Russian foreign trade. In fa
vorable seasons her export of wheat is large, mainly
from Black Sea ports. If the ports of Europe are
closed to her ships, in retaliation, her wheat export
must cease, and what the United States loses in her
direct trade will be more than made good by the re
moval of so formidable a competitor in the bread
stuff market. It may be the ukase is promulgated in
pique' at the failure of the Czar's peace conference, in
which the nations exhibited but little sympathy with
his disarmament proposition, and the effort was a
fiasco. If this is so he has made, practically, a decla
ration of commercial war, and a decree that Russia
shall stew in her own juice and go her own way in
disregard of the rest of the world. Among the inter
national sensations of the year this takes first rank
from a commercial point of view.
To return to San Francisco's interest in it. Our
direct trade with Russian Asia is largely in American
bottoms. Since September. 1808, there have cleared
from this port for Vladivostok the American ships
Lucelle, 1297 tons; Bonanza. 1309 tons; Welna. 1368
tons, and Columbia. 1327 tons. For Nicolaefski the
American ships Volunteer, 542 tons: Nokomis, 462
, tons, and the Vine, 222 tons. For Petropaulofski the
Russian ship Behring. 377 tons, and for Chemainus
the Russian steamer Dolney Vostok. 2265 tons. This
makes a total of 6617 American and 2642 Russian
tonnage cleared from San Francisco, or 9259 total
i tonnage. The employment of seven American ves
sels to those ports from this is not a large but is an
' encouraging item in our foreign trade, and should
1 move our shipping interests to make Government in
quiry into the effect of the ukase upon the treaty cf
! 1832 and our interests thereunder.
According tn some the accidents of the Southern
Pacific Railway are due to too much carelessness,
others say to too much economy, and still others to
j too much Kruitschnitt, and there are reasons for be
lieving if the road were rid of the latter it would expe
j rience less of the other two.
MR. JOHNSON'S HARD TASK
JOHN G. JOHNSON of Kansas is not as yet
a man of national fame, but if he survive the
blizzards of the fall and be not lost in the snow
drifts of winter he is likely to be a prominent and
conspicuous figure in politics along about the begin
ning of next spring, for he has been chosen execu
tive officer of the Democratic National Committee by
the element in favor of harmony, and it will be hid
duty to establish peace in the camp and bring back
'< the revolters of 1896.
It is strange that a man from Johnson's State
should have been chosen as a harmonizer, for Kansas
and harmony are not an accordant combination.
Had it been designed to have a cyclone executive
1 officer who would sweep the country for the career
ing and windy candidate from Nebraska his selec
tion would have been more readily understood. It
is probable, however, that by reason of his long deal
ings with the turbulent factions of. Democracy and
Populism in Kansas Mr. Johnson may have learned
the value of harmony and by practice acquired some
skill in effecting it. At any rate he may be expected
to have a knowledge of the best means of herding up
stampeded steers, and therefore in choosing him for
their leader in the National Committee the friends of
harmony may not have acted so absurdly as most peo
ple will be apt to believe.
The first task set before the new executive officer
is that of harmonizing the Democrats of Kentucky
and bringing the decent element among them to the
support of the regular ticket in order to assure the
election of Goebel as Governor and the re-election of
Senator Blackburn. It will be seen that Mr. John
son's fitness for the work of reuniting national Dem
ocracy has at the outset been subjected to a test of no
ordinary difficulty. The fight in Kentucky has been
one of such an exhausting nature that orators of
repute have been known to collapse in an effort to do
justice to the subject and fall fainting on the stump.
He who quiets that racket will certainly have attested
his right to be intrusted with the still more difficult
task of getting the Palmer and Buckner veterans of
1896 to train in the camp and keep step in the march
with the Bryanites in 1000.
San Jose is discussing the advisability of celebrat
ing with a grand festival the fiftieth anniversary of
the organization of the State government of Califor
nia, which took place in that city December 20, 1849;
and while the event would crowd the Christmas holi
days a little it is probable everybody will have
enough money to join in the jubilee, and fairly cer
i tain they will have a good time if they do.
THE FILIPINO INSURGENTS.
THE imperialist press, in concert, is harping
loudly about Aguinaldo and his relations to the
treaty of Biaknabato. These have been so often
established on the testimony of John Barrett, Consul
Wildman and other imperialists that to continue mis
representing them is puerile. To publish the truth of
history is not to support or defend Aguinaldo. It
is quite remarkable that the imperialist press pursues
exactly the policy for which it impeaches the Filipino
chief. He is called a mercenary adventurer, who sold
his cause to Spain sordidly for gold. His people
have no civilization, and no unity of purpose. He is
a liar and sustains his cause by misrepresentation,
and so on through an endless expletive and epitheti
cal discussion, every substantial statement of which
is flatly contradicted by the facts of history as pub
lished, personally or officially, by United States Con
suls Pratt, Wildman and Williams, and by Dewey,
Professor Schurman, General Miller, General Reeve,
Lieutenant Stickney and the united corps of Manila
correspondents of American newspapers.
The latest contribution to the confusion of the im
perialist press is an article in the August number of
Harper's Magazine, by Lieutenant Calkins of the
navy, who was with Dewey on the Olympia in the
battle of Manila Bay. Between the destruction of
Montijo's fleet and the fall of Manila, and for some
time thereafter, Lieutenant Calkins enjoyed excep
tional opportunities of intercourse with the Filipino
government and people, and became personally ac
quainted with Aguinaldo and his civil and military
officers. He had access to the Spanish archives and
records, and from them compiled material for his ar
ticle on "The Filipino Insurrection of 1896," to which
we call attention.
The imperialist press of this country speaks of it a;,
an outbreak of bandits, which Spain was preparing
to finally crush when we appeared in the islands to
continue and complete her work. Calkins describes
the work we are expected to complete. In one band,
shot to death by Spain, knelt "two millionaires, the
village doctor, lawyer and schoolmaster." In another
man-hunt the Spanish records note the capture and
slaughter of planters, proctors, magistrates, a school
master, a millionaire, and a dramatic poet, "the inso
lent translator into Tagal of the works of Ernest
Renan." After Blanco was furnished an increase of
25.000 soldiers they were hailed by the Spaniards and
banqueted. Don Rafael Comenge, a high official of
the Department of Justice, addressing them, said:
"You are just in time. The cannibals are still in the
woods; the wild beasts arc hiding in their lairs; the
hour has come to exterminate the savages; ferocious
animals must be killed: noxious herbs must be rooted
out; show no mercy; destroy, kill; grant no pardon,
for that is the prerogative of the King, not of the
army. Admit no overtures of peace in the face of
this treason." From these gentle creature!^ it seems
we bought the right to continue their extermination
of the bandits! Blanco was succeeded by Polavieja
as Captain General, who instructed the army that
"rebellion must be wiped out in blood." Then the
public executions began and continued until even the
bloodthirsty Spanish spirit sickened and they were
ordered to take place behind prison walls. Among
the "bandits" thus exterminated was Dr. Rizal, a
graduate and medaler of Spanish and German uni
versities, long the authority in Vienna on ocular de
eases, their surgery and treatment, the author of
"Noli me Tangere" and other books. He was
charged with having agitated for Philippine indepen
dence since his nineteenth year, and his books written
between 1888 and 1892 were produced in evidence
against him. He was led out on the fatal field of
Bagumbayan, and refusing to kneel, was shot to
death.
After the treaty and the banishment of Aguinaldo,
Lieutenant Calkins says, that, suspecting the faith of
Spain, Aguinaldo kept the revolutionary council and
their fund, the money paid to him by Spain, well in
hand, and the money was used to purchase arms for the
conquest of CaVite province in May. 1898, "that cam
paign having been made with arms purchased abroad
or captured from the Spanish troops, and not with the
remnants which the Filipinos were allowed to take
from the arsenal after its capture by Admiral Dewey."
Lieutenant Calkins' article is kept far within the
limit of reserve that must be respected by a navy of
ficer, but Americans reading it will feel little pride
in the purchase by this country of the Spanish right
to exterminate the "bandits" of the Philippines.
Having tried Dreyfus by a military court, the
French now begin another sensation by trying
twenty-two politicians before the Senate, a species of
political court, and we shall now see whether in her
many trials that are to come France can look with
most hope to her army or her Senate.
Sheriff Langford of Santa Clara County has
started out again in search of the bones of Murderer
Dunham. His prospects of success this time seem
to be particularly good by reason of the fact that lie
lias taken the precaution to carry a number of skele
ton keys with him.
Tom Reed's farewell letter to his constituents was
most excellent in every respect except that it did not
give the slightest intimation of the reason why he
bade them farewell.
"WE HAVE SCOTCH'D THE SNAKE, NOT KILLED IT."
DEALING WITH THE DIVORCE PROBLEM.
Different Ways in Which Churches and
States Regard It.
SINCE the marriage of Mrs. Sloane to O. H. P. Belmont the clergy both in
the Eastern and Western States have shown a disposition to be more cau
tious in solemnizing marriages. The General Association of Congrega
tional Ministers of Connecticut has passed resolutions declaring that it is
Incumbent on ministers to exercise the greatest caution before consenting
to officiate at the marriage of any person who has been divorced, and "that such
consent should never bo given in case of the guilty party divorced" for infidelity,
nor for any other reason, without a careful examination Into all the facts. The
Rev. W. M. Barrows of Greenwich, who had accepted a fee of $500 for the Bel
mont-Sloane marriage but had subsequently returned it, claiming that he had
been deceived, was not, however, condemned by the association.
A case somewhat similar to this Connecticut marriage occurred recently in
Michigan. Two persons who, according to the laws of the commonwealth, had
the right to marry were refused by each of the evangelical ministers at Ann
Arbor, on the ground that one. of the applicants had been divorced. These
clergymen later united in an agreement not to perform the ceremony of mar
riage when either of the persons concerned had been divorced or had obtained
;i divorce from a preceding husband or wife on any other ground than violation
of the seventh commandment. In general, the religious press has expressed
approval of this action. Some secular papers, however, think this position
irrational in itself, and derogatory to the laws of the State. For example, the
Brooklyn Eagle says:
"Law itself contains reasons for divorce, equivalent in moral weight to the
reason of infidelity, though different from it. An unexplained absence of
seven years works presumption of death and makes a marriage voidable. So
does a sentence of a certain duration for felony, especially a sentence for
life, which establishes the presumption of civil death. So does proof of a
marriage by force or fraud, or proof that one of the persons to it has not at
taim-d the age of lawful consent. There are other causes which make the
hard-and-fast attitude of the Ann Arbor clergy difficult to maintain, and
would present so many exceptions as to embarrass the clergy there or any
where else.
'â¢Refusal of sundry of the clergy of Michigan to accept the laws of Michi
gan, by which marriageabiltty or remarriageability is allowed, might induce
the law-making power to take away from such clergymen the right to per
form any marriage ceremony at all. It is a legal right, a right conferred by
law, not a right inherent of itself in the ministerial office itself. In France,
any marriage may be ecclesiastically performed, but every marriage, includ
ing these ecclesiastically performed, must also be performed by a civil mag
istrate, else the union Is not legal and resultant children are not legitimate.
Jn America, either the religious or civil ceremony suffices; but marriages
in America, to be valid in France and in some other foreign coun
tries, must be performed before a magistrate here. On that account,
foreigners here are generally married by ministers and 'by magistrates.
Besides, I'nitartan and Universallst ministers, as a rule, take no such rigid
ground as that imputed to the evangelical clergy of Ann Arbor. Uniform
ity of marriage conditions or of attitude toward divorce conditions will not
be possible so long as every State can establish its own rules and so long
as ministers themselves construe their duty in the case for themselves."
A party in the Protestant Episcopal Church has long been endeavoring to
amend the canon concerning divorce by forbidding the solemnization of the
marriage "of any person who has a divorced husband or wife still living, if
such husband or wife has been put away for any cause arising after mar
riage." Bishop Seymour of the diocese of Quincy, 111., has announced his in
tention to offer the foregoing amendment at the next general convention at
San P'rancisco, In October, 1901. Of this the Christian Work says, however:
"There is no fear that the general convention will amend fhe canons Jn
this matter; their action last year is evidence on this point. And why should
the physical death of an Infamous and immoral wife and mother be neces
sary before a pure, good mother can be procured for a motherless family?
Bishop Seymour's proposition has neither reason nor the teaching of Christ
to commend it."
In the meantime the secular arm has also been trying to make the estate of
the divorced a hard one. Mr. Justice Beekman of the New York Supreme Court
has formulated a new plun to defeat "collusion" in divorce proceedings. Although
almost all European governments assume that when both husband and
wife are agreed that their union is no longer tolerant or subservient to
good, this is one of the best of all reasons for their divorce, the laws in this
country take just the opposite ground. If two people, who agree in nothing else,
agree that they must part in order to find life endurable, the legislative or
legal Dogberry feels sure in his soul that here Is "collusion," something particu
larly despicable in his eyes, and that this is the best of all reasons why the
state and church should combine to hold these two together. However, not all
legal students in this country agree with this view. Mr. S. N. Tuckman, writ
ing in the New York Times, says:
"Mr. Justice Beekman very properly .states that the family is the pillar of
the state, and the state by means of laws should preserve its sanctity. This
is very true in the abstract, but 1 am rather inclined to believe that the
husband and wife who cannot live happily together and therefore collude
to divorce are. if constrained to live together, more mischievous to the com
munity and exert a more immoral influence upon their home and children
than they would if they were divorced.
"Let us rather cling to the prosent law, and let matters of matrimony
be permitted to adjust and elevate themselves."
Mr. Allen Carruthers makes an Interesting comparison of the divorce laws of
Europe and the United States. He says:
"Austria grants divorce for 'unconquerable aversion,' on account of which
both parties ask for a divorce. Any court in the Union would dismiss the
petition as collusive where both parties agree to ask for the divorce.
"Hungary has the same law as Austria on this subject.
"Belgium grants divorces on 'mutual or persevering desire or consent of
both parties.'
"Denmark, 'mutual consent.'
"In almost all of the provinces of the German empire divorces are
granted for 'unconquerable aversion.'
" 'Agreement after five years' separation is a valid ground for divorce in
the Netherlands.
"Norway, 'mutual consent.'
"Roumanla, 'mutual and continued desire of both parties.'
"Russia, 'mutual consent.'
"Sweden, 'Incompatibility of temper and persistent discord.'
"Switzerland, which recently adopted a Federal divorce law and amended
the sume generally, grants divorces "where the marriage relations are 'greatly
strained'; also, 'incurable mental diseases of three years" standing,' 'injury to
the Bono* or reputation of either of the parties.'
"The causes just mentioned are far more liberal than any cause known In
America. In fact, some of the causes for which divorce is allowed in Europe
are reasons In most every State in the Union for refusing the divorce \s an
example, 'mutual consent.' While in many instances both parties may want
the divorce, yet if it were known by the court that the application was based
upon 'mutual consent' it would defeat the divorce every time in America Be
sides the causes Just mentioned as an example of the liberality of the divorce
laws of Europe, almost all of the countries of that continent have divorce laws
embracing about all of the causes that we have in America. A great many
are under the Impression that divorce can be obtained in America for 'incom
patibllity of temper.' Yet, as a matter of fact, there is no such cause for dl
vor<*e in any State in the Union."
Mr. Cnrruthers says that there is a marked Increase In divorce both in Furnns
and America, but that this can not be attributed to any fault of our laws for
"the people are back of the laws in every Instance in thin country " He conti'miU
"The report of the Commissioner of Labor referred to by Cardinal <Mh
bons. which was based upon a very careful and disinterested investirntliâ¢
plainly demonstrates that stringent or liberal laws of divorce do not cor r*.

Bertlllon, who is probably the ablest and most exhaustive writer unnn twÂ«
subiect. This Is also borne out by the statistics of various countries
"The fact is. we may Judge our divorce laws and also the number n f
divorces granted by the sentiment of the people. New Yor*. for in<Ua nil tl
the only State in the I nlon that grants divorco solely upon the Km, , ,i il
infidelity. Yet New York furnishes more divorce suits" than any other 4L?I
In the Union, and probably more unhappy marriagps. In this State mar
riage is easiest and divorce hardest of an> State in the Union mar

MAY TROT OUT
THE DARK HORSE
AT ANY MOMENT
Proposed Republican
Nominee for Mayor.
â â¢
The Republican "dark horse" for
Mayor may be brought forth to-day.
Henry T. Scott seemed to be confident
yesterday that the candidate whose
name is held in reserve would be made j
known this evening or to-morrow. "His
consent to make the race." said Mr. j
Scott, "has not been obtained, but I j
believe he will accept the nomination. \
In our marking of first choice he has !
received a majority. Each man attend- j
ing the conference writes his first i
choice of a candidate for Mayor, and i
this 'dark horse' leads the list."
The conference referred to by Mr. j
Scott is a council composed of twelve '
or fifteen citizens who have taken an [
active part in organizing the anti-boss ;
dements of the Republican party in the ;
city. The choice of the conference may
not be the choice of the convention, but
the indorsement of the men who are !
working so earnestly to pet the best ;
men of the city to serve the public is ;
sure to carry weight.
The Republicans are making progress j
in their efforts to induce merchants and j
businÂ»s3 men of known responsibility !
and experience to accept nominations i
for the Board of Supervisors. On the
other hand, the Democrats are mak- :
me but little headway in this direction.
The "old squaws" and the old fossils â 
are besieging the committee of seven
for favorable considei-ation. but the
men whosf 4 services are sought cannot
neglect their business affairs to serve
the city.
The Republicans acknowledge that
they cannot win the office of Mayor and
Assessor except by the hardest kind
of fighting and the presentation of Can
didates of exceptional strength. Last
evening it was ascertained that Super
visor Albert Heyer would accept the
nomination for Assessor. Mr. Heyer
has lived in San Francisco forty years,
and has never been defeated by the
people in an election. He has been a
candidate five times. Once he was
counted out. He is willing to make the
race for Assessor against Dr. Dodge,
and is confident of success at the poll?.
The aspirants for place on the ticket
as candidate for Sheriff are John
Lackmann. Colonel Duboce and Cap
tain Thomas O'Xell. For Auditor W.
G. Doane and Asa R. Wells are can
didates. The list of entries for Public
Administrator is growing. The leaders
in the contest are John Farnham, Will
iam Lyle and the incumbent, Mr.
Drinkh

by postal card gave the members no
tice of the proposed postponement.
Several members failed to receive the!
notice, hence some displeasure was i
manifested when they assembled at !
headquarters last night and found that
the meeting was off.

morrow evening.

LEGALITY OF
THE TAX LEVY
IS DISPUTED
County Officials Say
It is Invalid.
"Was the tax levy fixed on Monday by
the Supervisors invalidated by the fact
that the park fund was allowed but .0535
on the dollar instead of .06. as required
by statute? That question was discussed
at greater length yesterday by the pov
erty-stricken heads of municipal depart
ments and their employes than any mat
ter of business that came into the City
Hal! during the day. A rumor that the
levy was invalid was started on the
rounds early in the morning, and the
spirits of those whose salaries were
docked and whose appropriations were
cut to the minimum went up like a sum
mer resort thermometer. That there was
some foundation for the belief was dem
onstrated by a glance at the statutes.
The particular clause which is intended
to assure funds for park purposes reads
as follows:
â â¢The Board of Supervisors. Municipal
Council or other legislative body of any
city or city and county in the State, hav
ing' within its limits parks and ground*
under the control and management of a
Board of Park Commissioners and of an
area or acreage of more than ten acres.
la hereby authorized and empowered to
ievy and collect each year in the mode
prescribed by law for the levy and col
lection of taxes a tax of not less than 6
nor more than 10 cents upon each $100 on
the assessed valuation of taxable prop
erty within such city or city and county.
tor the purpose of preserving, maintaln
.ng and Improving the parks and grounds
under the control of such Board of Park
Commissioners."
To this section the Park Commissioners
called the attention of the Board of Su
pervisors when it submitted its first levy
but when the levy adopted by the board
on Monday was considered on Sunday by
the Mayor and Supervisor Perrault they
â lecided to ignore it and fix a lower rig
ure. They contend now that even if the
aw made"it mandatory upon the board to
set aside 6 cents for park purposes the
whole levy would not be Invalidated If it
failed to do so.
In that opinion the most prominent at
torneya in the city agree. District At
torney Murphy said last night that it was
a matter upon which he would refuse to
clve an offhand opinion, but that he could
hot conceive of any such result arising
out of a slight dereliction of duty on the
r.art of the Supervisors. Garret McEner
hey was of the same opinion. E. S. Pills
burv looked well Into his law books and
rendered the same decision. The attor
neys were a unit on that point, and had
only a few of the Supervisors and heads
jf departments to disagree with them.
The point is one that has never been
tested in the courts, and probably will
not te on this occasion, as the commis
sion most affected, the Park Commission,
ha* announced through Mr. Zeile that it
will take advantage of no such opportu
nity to raise the money it deems neces
sary for park purposes. However, any of
the well-slashed heads or any taxpayer
may throw th- matter into court and
-ettle the question once and for all.

fund of St. Francis Church, of which Rev.

Stockton streets. Tuesday evening, Octo
ber 3. The chief feature of the entertain

which will be exhibited by Mr. James W.
Erwin.

California Volunteers, will lecture later
In Metropolitan Temple. This will be
Father McKlnnon'a great effort, and he
will then deal exclusively with the war hi
the Philippines.

Thousands Suffer From It
Without Knowing Its Real
Character. i

remedies; the real seat of mischief Is lost !
sight of; the stomach is the organ to be

stomach weakness. Nervous dyspepsia

cases the heart palpitates and is irreg- !
ular: in others the kidneys are affected; j

loss of flesh and appetite, with the accu-

burn.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver unsettled. Exchange remains the same.
Wheat neglected and nominal. Freights firmly held.
Farley very dull, zvith ample offerings.
Oats, Corn and Rye as previously reported.
Choice Hay steady; medium and lower grades depressed.
No change in Feedstuff s.
New Beans coming in slowly. Seeds unchanged.
es depressed. Tomatoes in heavy supply.
Heavy receipts of Butter from the north.
Cheese and Eggs weak.
Poultry kept weak b\ large receipts from the East.
Wine Crapes arrive sparingly and bring fine prices.
Lemons and Limes scarce and firm.
ns doing heifer.

Prices for new crop Walnuts expected this week.
Provisions quieted down again.
Wool. Hides and Tallow firm. Hops dull.
Hogs easy at the decline. Beef and Mutton firm again.

Oils stand as before quoted.

"WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has fallen slowly over the coun
try west of the Rocky Mountains, except alon?
the ast, where there has been a slight rise.
An area of low pressure of the usual summer
type overlies Arizona and Southeastern Cali
fornia.
The temperature has fallen slowly along thÂ»
coast, and conditions arc favorable for dense
fog along the entire coast of California. The
weather has continued favorable for raisin
making in the vicinity of Fresno.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, September 20. 1593:
For Northern Californiaâ Fair Wednesday;
not quite ho warm In the interior; southerly
â winds inland; fresh westerly winds on the
coast with fog.
For Southern Californiaâ Fair Wednesday;
light northwesterly winds.
For Nevada â Fair Wednesday; cooler.
For Utahâ Fair Wednesday.
For Arizonaâ Fair Wednesday.
For Pan Francisco and vicinity â
Wednesday; cooler; fresh westerly winds with
fog In the afternoon.
ALEXANDER G. McADIE.
Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS
New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK, Sept. 19.- The delicate tension
of tlio speculative tfmper was Illustrated by
the course of to-day's market, which seemed
to he going prosperously on Its way toward
ration and recovery In prices until tho
If hour of the trading. After that time
prices were bowled over In every direction,
]ik>- :t set of ten pins which had been set up
i be knocked down attain. The st- 11-
Ing movement waa in full force when the
marki I I i>rlces tending rapidly down-
Borne â  f the earlier Rains were held
ih'Hi^h In other prominent stocks the notable
leved earlier in the day was most
i. wiped out. The market pave every appear
r having thrown off the Incubus of the
money scare. The reports from abroad were
of n more conciliatory attitude on the part of
lent Kruger, and the decision of the
nt to pardon Dreyfus caused
:; rally in foreign markets. There as well as
the phort Interest had been largely over
ded and they found some difficulty in
securing Itoclci to cover short contracts. Pro
al operators who hnd secured stocks at
lay's low level had turned hull and there
was a fair volume of buying in commission
| houses, attracted by yesterday's lute recovery
in prices. Prices were marked high at the
opening and trading was very active for a
time, but on a descending scale. After prices
had got back to near yesterday's close a
i demand of substantial character developed for
I standard railroad stocks. Some of the special
ties, notably the New York traction stocks,
! which have suffered violent losses, were
I marked up rapidly. Late in the day a very
1 buoyant tone carried the coalers upward under
â  the lead of Laekawanna, which advanced an
extreme 114. Other members of the group
rose from 24 to 4 points. Metropolitan's ex
treme rise was 64. Brookly Transit was up at
] one time 4%. Many other specialties were up
I between 2 and 3 points, and the active rail-
I roads very generally showed gains of from
1 to IV Such was the state of affairs when
traders began to take profits in the coalers
and Metropolitan Street Railway. It did not
! take the bears long to wipe out all of the
gains In Brooklyn Transit, and the course of
prices turned quickly downward. The selling
movement continued actively until the close.
The advance in prices was accompanied by pro
fessions of confidence that the money market
difficulties had been passed. Call money ruled
at 6 per cent and above during the day. The
banks are not losing quite as heavily as last
week, either to the sub-treasury or to the in
terior. But the outward movement of money
' is more than sufficient to make Inroads upon
' the legal reserve of the banks. It Is known
i that many loans which have been called have
; been placed with other borrowers or have re
sulted simply in a shifting of collaterals while
the loan was marked up but I? ft otherwise
undisturbed. Reports found currency that con
siderable money had been placed in call loans
for account of Canadian banks. One New-
York bank took out additional circulation in
Washington to the amount of 1250,000, which
notes had already been printed and mad*
1 available for circulation. Except in these
cases there Is no evidence of any actual re
plenishment of cash for the New York market,
nor is any discernahle in the Immediate future.
Bankers express the oponion that money will
continue hard at 6 per rent or above. One
of the vagaries of the situation is that sterling
exchange held stead'- to firm to-day with buy-
Ing for London account of over 18. 000 shares
of stocks, -while it dropped sharply yesterday
In spite of London's selling of over 40.000
shares. Competent authorities do not look for
gold imports until much later In the fall.
Bonds showed gains and held better than
stocks In the late weakness. Total sales, par
value, 12,290,000.
United States bonds were unchanged in bid
quotations.

Chicago Grain Market.
CHICAGO, Sept. 19.â The opening: In Wheat
was at a slight advance for December, that
option starting at 704'8<70<S,c, or K0!4o oorv *r
yesterday's final price. Outside of an unex
pectedly small Increase In the local contract
stocks there' was nothing In the news to war
rant an advance, and It appeared to be purely
the result of local sentiment. The small ad
vance was almost Immediately lost. Heavy
selling, which marked yesterday's session and
which was supposed to be liquidation of some
long lines, was continued to-day. There was
also some selling from the outside. St. Louis
and the Northwest both being In the market.
For a considerable period the December price
hung around TO^fe'aWic, to which point It de
clined shortly after the opening, but before n
o'clock the strength of Corn began to attract
attention. Bullishness caused by this was In
creased by reports that a good deal of the
buying was for Armour. This scared shorts
and caused covering, offerings were light,
outside of the long liquidation referred to, and
prices fluctuated rapidly within a compara
tively narrow range, the market at times ex
hibiting some nervousness. The local crowd
was apparently long, and though the posting
of Bradstreet's figures on the visible supply,
ehowlnjj an increase of 6,098,000 bushels, shook
out prime long Wheat, offerings were readily
absorbed and the spell of weakness soon dis
appeared. During the afternoon the strength
of coarse grains was influential in the steadi
ness of Wheat, and although bear attacks were
frequent, prices were well maintained to the
end. L,ittle waa done in September. Interest
In that deal had apparently died out and prices
kept close to the opening priceâ 7o%câ most of
the session. December was bringing 70% cat
the close.
Trading In Corn was quite active, especially
for September, in which a sharp advance was
recorded. Shorts were active bidders from the
start, but had a good deal of difficulty in
covering and prices were bid up accordingly.
December closed %ft^c higher. September
gained l^c.
Oats were helped by the strength of Corn
and ruled firm all day, December closing 14c
higher.
Provisions were dull and steady, sympathy
with the grain markets being the main factor.
There wan a good demand for meats. Pack
ers were ">n both sides of the market. Frl^s
kept within a narrow rani;e. At the close
January Perk and Laird were a shade higher
and rose 2%c higher.

Northern Wheat Market
OREGON.
PORTLAND, Or., Sept. 19.â Portland export
ers have been forroi! out of the market by
high-priced wheat and high-priced ships. Either
one or the other might have been met sepa
rately, but combined they have brought mat
ters to a ntandstill. San Francisco men are
chartering ships to go from the north to the
Bay city at a figure which would mean an
actual loss of 3 or 4 cents per bushel if Port
land dealers were to attempt to fill them here.
At the lowest rate Intimated for a ship in this
port. Walla Walla would not be worth m.jre
than r.S cents per bushel, but very little can
be purchased at that figure. In fact, some
sales of heavy weight are mnde at less than
f.9 and 60 cents. Valley Is in much better con
dition, nominally COiiieic. Bluestem Is quoted
at 61c.

Butter, Cheese and Eggs.
There were heavy receipts of Butter from
the mirth yesterday, but as a good deal of it
had been placed prior to arrival the market
held up. The weakness Is confined to the
medium and lower grades, as strictly fancy
creamery is in light supply.
Cheese and Eggs are in ample supply and
weak, r.r.d it takes a flue Egg to bring the top
quotation. Most of the buyers are running on
Eastern.

Dried Fruits, Nuts, Raisins.
More steadiness in Prunes is reported, but
they are still very dull, and some outside lots
are being offered at 2%c, though but few Santa
Claras can be obtained as low as 3c basis.
Prunes are unusually dull thus far this year.
Pears are scarce and stiff. Peaches, con
trary to anticipations, are beginning to pick
up and show more firmness with an Improving
demand. Apricots are stiff, of course. Apples
are weak.
Prices on new crop Walnuts are expected this
week. Almonds continue very firm.
The feeling in Raisins is firm and the grow
ers are strong holders. Honey is quiet and fea
tureless.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Mining stocks weakened off and the best
prices were generally lower than on Monday.
The telegram fiom the pump said: "Stopped
No. 2 elevator frnm 1 p. m. to 7 p. m. yes
terday, while putting Improved screens In the
supply tank. The water rose during the
stoppage 23 feet and 2 Inches. The elevator
was running well at 6:30 this morning. The
water is 74 feet 3 inches below the 1950-foot
level station."
There was a fair trade in local securities,
and Giant Powder sold down to $73 25. Oceanto
Steamship sold at $88 75.
Dividends of 25 cents and -42 corns per rhari
will be paid by the Hutchinaon Sugar and
Spring Valley Water companies to-day.
The Western Union Telegraph Company has
declared a quarterly dividend of IVi per cent,
payable on October 16.
A quarterly dividend of fl per share is now
payable by the Eastern Dynamite Company.

the city front (Mission-street wharf) about
twenty-five minutes later than at Fort Point;
he height of tide is the same at both places.
NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
th* early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of tha
day in the order of occurrence as to time. Tha
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
'nst tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are In addition to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters

Rumor of Rottenness in Government
Contracts Is Circulated.
The charge has been unofficially made
that the Government has been paying
more for the oil necessary to lubricate
the machinery in the army transports
than is warranted by the prices ruling in
the market. No proof, other than an in
terview with a local dealer, who has been
unsuccessful in securing another con
tract, has been submitted. That inter
view Is from Charles M. Vates of Yan-s
& Co.. which firm furnishiHl oil at 60
cents a gallon for the transports Han
cock Grant and Sherman, the Standard
Oil Company now having the contract at
32 cents a gallon. In that interview he
said:
I am not going to make any charges about
the trouble over the <>!! we furnished the
transport Hancock. There was a complaint
made to the quartermaster's department
and I went to see Colonel Long at nnce. It
appears that the chief engineer said In a
mport that the oil wan too light for the
heavy machinery of that vessel. That ig a
matter which could easily l>e rectified, and I
so explained to Colonel Long. I heard no
more after that, but I see we did not gtt
any more contracts. In tart, the next thing
I heard was that the Standard Oil Company
had been successful In securing a contract
for "1000 barrels, mÂ»ne or leHB." as the
tenders read, at 32 cents a gallon.
Colonel Long, the head of the quarter
master's department, refuses to make
any statement on the matter, as doea \V.

Company. It is said, however, that the
new contract, which Yates did not get,

Names Found Drowned in the
Bay.
Tho decomposed remains of an old man
in tlu> garb qf a laborer were found yes
terday afternoon floating in the bay near
the foot of Uroadway wharf 2.
The body was taken to the Morgue and
the clothing was examined for a clew to
his identity. In the pockets were found a
deposit book on the Hibcrnia Savings
Bank in the name of Dennis Cunningham
and showing a balance of $41 76 In his
favor. There were also a punched meal
ticket "ii the Philadelphia House on Geor
gia street. Vallejo, John Plagemann, man
ager. The ticket is In the name of Wil
liam Kelly. There was also a receipt for
$1 in the name of William Kelly for lodg
ings and signed by Plagemann.
Among the papers was a letter from
John W. Morris, late principal examiner
of the renslon bureau at Washington,
addressed to William Kelly at the Phila
delphia House, Vallejo, In which the fol
lowing occurs: "The charges of desertion
standing against your record as of the
Vnitfd State? navy cannot be removed,
as there is now no existing law on the
subject."

HER HOME-MADE
REMEDY NEARLY
DROVE HIM MAD
Pantoskey Answers
His Wife's Charges.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Sept. 19.
The sensational charges of cruelty and I
Intemperance made by Rica Pantoskey
against her husband. Herman Pantoskey,
in her suit for the recovery of $75 month
ly for her maintenance and support, have
been met in an interesting and amusing i
answer and cross-complaint tiled by the
defendant this afternoon through his at
torney, D. Mitchell.
After denying each and every specific
charge of inflicting grievous bodily in
jury and mental Buffering upon the plain
tiff, or that he reviled her with the ap
probrious name of "thief" and "igno
ramus," and that she is destitute of
means, the aged defendant avers that
while he is possessed of some real estate,
it is mortgaged for $530, and that his
place of business is worth not over $150.
He denies that he has money on deposit
in banks, or that he has any intention
of disposing of bis earthly goods. He
denies also that there was any necessity
of bringing the suit, except for the pur
pose of Rica's "personal gain, revenge
and aggrandizement." For his second de
fense Pantoskey alleges:
''That he is 70 years of age, and for
two years last past has been and now
is suffering from mental and physical ail
ments, and therefore it became and was
an advertised necessity that he should
take some one to assist and care for him
In his age and infirmities.
"That plaintiff Rica, being without a
home or means of support and learning
of Herman's supposed wealth and great
ness, 'came from afar' not laden with
her jewels and precious stones, as she
had previously left same with her uncle,
and Bhe then offered him her love, heart
and hand and became his bride in Jan
uary, 1899; and as a dutiful wife prom
ised to love, honor and obey and to care
fully nurse defendants property and per
son Kut said plaintiff has not kept said
promise, but has willfully broken and de
parted ih'Tefrom."
The cross-complaint further alleges that
in February last the bride of one short
month "upon learning that defendant
was much indebted, requested that he
should turn over to her all his property
and I hereby defeat his creditors, and also
simultaneously demanded that her adult
son Simon Bendlt (an inmate of a house
of correction for felony), should on his re
lease therefrom come and reside with
plaintiff and defendant in their home in
Oakland and that he be by defendant
Bupported and established In their home,
although he is abl< -bodied, aged 27 years,
and well able to care for himself."
\ho\it the same time, according to the
cross-complaint, "Rica demanded a large
sum of money with which to release her
jewelry alleged to be pawned in San Fran
cisco by her or her Bon Simon." Herman's
vigorous refusal to me<-t these demands
resulted In the wife becoming aggressive,
disappointed and insulting until she finally

OAKLAND, Sept. 19.â Judge Greene this

MRS. WALSH'S DEATH.
Coroner Hill Issued a Warrant for
the Arrest of Dr. Kohl and Mrs.
Dr. Kohl.
Coroner Hill Issued a warrant yesterday
morning- for the arrest of John Doe and
Jane Doe on a charge of manslaughter,
the victim being Mrs. Katie Walsh, who
died at the Brunswick House <>n Sixth
street last Monday morning from the ef
fects of a criminal operation.
The testimony brought out the fact that
Mrs. "Walsh had been furnished pills by
Mrs. Dr. Kohl of 1122 Market street and
that Mr. Dr. Kohl, her husband, was
called in afterward to save the patient's
lit. . which he failed to do. The jury,
however, brought in a verdict to the ef
fect that the deceased came to her death
at the hands of parties unknown to the
jury.
A pubpena had been issued for the
Kohls to attend the inquest and testify in
thf case, but they could not be found.
Coroner Hill informed the jurors that
he could not approve of their verdict.
"The evidence is sufficient to show that
the deceased obtained the medicine from
Mrs. Dr. Kohl," he said.
The warrant was placed In the hands of
the police, with instructions to arrest Dr.
Kohl and Mrs. Dr. Kohl. The name of
the latter Is found in the medical register
under the heading of Illegal practitioners.

DEER PARK AS
AN INSPIRATION
FOR PROPOSALS
Millionaire Tells How
He Was Wooed.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 19.
John Nicholl, the aged Alameda County
capitalist, took the stand in his own be
half this afternoon in Judge Ogden's court
and offered testimony whijih hQÂ£ attorneys
are confident will squash Miss Hodge's
breach of promise suit. Previous to put
ting Xicholl on the stand McKee made his
opening statement to the jury, in which
he affirmed that the defense, among other
things, expected to prove that Nicholl is
physically and mentally much enfeebled.
Mr. McKee's client did not make good
the mental end of his attorney's suue
meni. Physically the old man is not in
good condition. The heavy stick he car
ries Is not a matter of fashion, but of
necessity. He hobbled up to the witness
stand and his whole bearing plainly
shewed his 77 years. Physically he Is
weak, but it is an Injustice to the vigor
ous old man to speak of feeble-minded
ness. Mr. Nicholl needed no prompting ro
remember dates nor ligurts, nor conver
sation, nor the contents of letters he
either received or dictated. He has a
gond memory and a sound brain, and he
showed it in spite of his counsel.
According to his story, he did not woo
and win Miss Hodge. She courted him
and got his consent, on conditions, to wed
her. "On the 10th of November," the wit
ness said, "I took Miss Hodge out riding.
AYe went out Fourth avenue to the top of
the ridge, east to Thirteenth street, on to
Diamond Canyon and past the Dlngee
place. We stopped alongside the place
and looked over and through the fence at
some deer there. Then she said, '1 have
an old dear alongside me in the buggy.'
Previous to that she had been hugging
up to me and calling me darling. During
the ride Jane talked of Mrs. Boyd,"
Nicholl went on to explain, "and of her
property. She said the Griffin crowd was
striving to win the affection of Mrs. Boyd
from her so as to get the Washington
street property. 'If Aunty Boyd should die
without a will.' she said, 'it would be a
year before anything could be done, and
during that time she (Jane) would have
no home."
It was while dolefully reflecting upon
her homeless condition that Miss Hodge
proposed to Nicholl. The lady wasted no
endearing terms, but stated her case
clearly and said: "If you would take me
over to your home I would make It so
pleasant you would be astonished." "But
the girls?" said the old man she was pro
posing to; "they will probably kick."
"I'll see the girls." said Miss Hodge.
"All right." said Nicholl: "if you get the
consent of the girlsâ otherwise no." Miss
Hodge did not get the consent of the girls.
"On the 7th of December." testified
Nicholl, "I went to see Jane. I told her
my daughter, Mrs. Wilson, was making
such a fuss about the engagement and
saying she was ashamed of me and
ashamed of Jane. 'If they are kicking so
much,' she said, 'it is all off and you can
go and tell the girls.' "
Mr. Nioholl on his cross-examination
showed that he had come to California In
UNITED STATES BRANCH.

outfit.

the engagement on November 12. Mrs.
Boyd told him, saying, "Guess who is
going to get married."

Nicholl girls had Bent for her and told
her of their father's engagement to Misa
Hodge, saying they would rather have
him marry hÂ°r than a stranger.

gagement and noticed in Mr. Nicholl con
duct becoming an engaged man.

RUMOR CREATES A SENSATION
IN FINANCIAL CIRCLES.
The rumor current In this city yester
day afternoon that the Crockers had dis
posed of their entire holdings in the
.Southern Pacific and Central Pacific
Hailroad companies to the backing firm
of Speyer & Co., of New York, created
intense excitement In financial circles.
While no positive confirmation of the
Btory was obtained, the source of in
formation is reliable, and It was generally
accepted as correct.
What lends color to the rumor is the
fact that neither George nor Will Crocker
would discuss the matter and refused to
affirm or deny it. When George Crocker
was asked whether the report was true
or not he quickly replied:
"Go ask Mr. Speyer. Probably he will
tey you," and he positively declined to
Bay any more about it.
Will Crocker was just as averse to dis
cussing the subject as his brother, and to
aJI Questions regarding it answered that
he did not care to speak about affairs
which he considered private and of no
possible public concern.
The firm of Speyer & Co. is widel>
known and has houses In Amsterdam ,
London and New York. It has en
gineered some very important financial
deals for C. P. Huntington, and recently
put through the famous reorganization
of the Central Pacific and its consolida
tion with the Southern Pacific. James
Speyer. a representative of the concern,
was" in this city a few months ago, and
it was probably at that time that he had
the Crocker deal in view. It is said that
the linn has placed over $L!o,0uu,000 in
bonds for Huntlngton. and it counts
among its patrons some of the wealthiest
people in Europe.
It was impossible to ascertain for whom
the Speyers were acting in the purchase of
the Crocker interests. It is supposed, how
ever, that they were acting lor some for
midable money power, and the names or
the Vanderbilts and even Huntlngton
himself are mentioned in this connection.
It is understood that all of the Crockers
have disposed of their stock. There are
five Of them and they together own a
fifth Interest In the property. It is
valued at about ?13.w0,00'J, and comprises
34U.U00 shares of the stock.
The Call's correspondent in New lorK
saw James Speyer last night in that city
and asked him if the story was correct
that he had purchased the Crocker In
terests in the Central and Southern Pa
cine Railway corporations. Mr. opeyer
declined to discuss the subject. ihe
Crockers here in San Francisco decline
to be interviewed on the topic. It is sig
nificant that none of the parties con
cerned deny the reported transaction.
James Speyer. president of the London,
Amsterdam and New York banking insti
tution of Speyer & Co., was in this city
last April. While here he purchased Mrs.
Stanford's holdings of Central Pacific
-tuck. He attended the meeting of Cen
tral Pacific dirtctors to arrange for the
absorption of the Central by the South
ern Pacific, and was accepted as C. P.
Jluntmgioii's ally in the reorganization
B Â°Chauncey M. Depew, who was seen in
New York last night by a Call ccrre
. ni. asserted positively that the
A anderbilts have nothing to do with the
Southern Pacific Kail way.
CALIFORNIANS AS HOSTS.
Fair Ladies of Company L En
tertain the Minnesotans.
To show their appreciation of the kind
ness extended to their brothers, sons and
husbands in Manila the lady relatives of
the men of Company L, gave a noonday
breakfast last Saturday to the members
of Company L. of the Minnesota regiment.
The affair took place in the company din
ing roorii, which had been turned over
to the fair hostesses, by whom it had
been converted into a bower of Lucullan
loveliness. A profusion of flowers decor
ated the table, and if here and there the
frosted top ot a cake or the glistening
neck of a large cold bottle happened to
i poke its way through the petals of the
' posies the tact detracted nothing from
: the loveliness of the ensemble as viewed
by the hungry eyes of the warriors from
the battlefields of Luzon.
At the appointed hour the California
boys marched over to the Minnesota camp
and escorted back the guests that there
awaited them. Over two hundred men sat
down to the table and made havoc of the
: things with which it was loaded,
while their gentle entertainers hovered
over them and took Rood care that all
thfir wants should be promptly admin
istered to.
At thr- end of the meal Captain Eggert
arose and on behalf of the men or his
company bade the Minnesota contingent
welcome In a few well chosen and appro
priate words in which he endeavored to
convey to the guests the deep apprecia
tion with which the courtesies they had
extended to the men from Frisco while In
Manila bad been received. When he con
cluded Captain Morgan arose and an-
BWered for himself and the men of his
company. Then came cheers for the
hostesses, the different companies, the
officers and for everything else, after
which cigars were lighted and all sat at
ease to listen to the songs. Jests and sto
ries that wore away the better part of
the afternoon.
Among the ladles who contributed to
make the affair a success were Mrs. Oap
tJiin Etrfcert, Mrs. Lieutenant Grundman,
the Misses Adler. Mrs. Meyers, Mrs.
Kahn. Mrs. Grant. Mrs. Mangles, Mrs.
LefTman, Mrs. Burke, Mrs. S. Aufrichtlg
and a number of others whose names
could not be learned.

dishes and properly cooled bottles.

SPEYER BOUGHT
A BIG BLOCK
OF THE STOCK
NEW YORK, Sept. 19.â The Journal to
day says: A large share in the Central
and Southern Pacific railways has been
bought by Speyer & Co., bankers of this
city.
It was said last night by a gentleman
conversant with the affairs of the two
companies that Speyer & Co. had ar
ranged recently to purchase a large biock
of these securities but he could not tell
whether the Interest acquired was the
Crocker or the Stanford holdings.
The Journal's informant, however,
said he could deny the report that the
deal was partly a Vanderbilt scheme for
the control of the transcontinental rail
way system.
"There is no Vanderbilt money In this,"
he continued. "Mr. Speyer has been very
friendly to C. P. Huntlngton. The firm
put through the reorganization of the
Central Pacific. Mr. Speyer is not buy
ing, as I understand it, for Mr. Huntlng
ton, but is taking over, as opportunity
offers, the holdings of others who are
not in sympathy with Mr. Huntington's
Interests, and will hold them on his own
account."
Mr. Speyer was in San Francisco a
short time ago, and it was understood at
that time that a large deal in Central
Pacific was in the wind.
The Sun says: Senator Depew said last
night that the Vanderbilts had no inter
ests in any such deal. James Speyer,
head of the firm of Speyer & Co., refused
to discuss the report. He has recently
returned to town. There has been talk in
Wall street lately about an "important
move" coming in the stock. It has been
dealt In heavily of late, the price ranging
between 40 and 35. About 30.000 shares
were sold yesterday, the closing price
being 35%.
whistle and Lloyd Spencer with his coon
songs contributed more than a little to
make the good time what it was.
TO WELCOME OTHERS.
A meeting of the citizens' executive
committee ''on the reception of California
volunteers from Manila on board the
Sherman was held in Mayor Phelan's
office yesterday afternoon. There were
present: Mayor Phelan, M. H. de Young,
W. J. Martin and Secretary McPlke.
During the session of the committee a
delegation from the parents' association
consisting of J. W. Burson, J. R. Baird,
L, N. Jacobs, J. J. McCarthy and E. C.
Brackett called and requested that ar
rangements be made for the reception
and banqueting of the returning regulars
now on the way from Manila on board
the City of Para, its hospitalities to be
extended to all of the men who belong
to California and who enlisted in other
regiments, as though they had belonged
to the First Regiment of Volunteers, and
the reception to partake, if possible, of a
naval parade on a small scale. The com
mittee decided to give the returning
soldiers a breakfast in the east nave of
the ferry building, provided the Harbor
Commissioners would permit.
After disposing of this the committee
settled down to render an account of the
financial feature of the late reception.
The following was issued to the press
representatives present:
There are still a number of unsettled bills in
the hands of yuur secretary, but In accordance
with your request we submit to you the fol
lowinK statement ol muney already paid out.
The bills now on hand amount to about $Ijoo.

COMES TO MEET HIS
NORTHERN FIGHTERS
GOVERNOR ROBERT B. SMITH, MONTANA'S EXECUTIVE.
GOVERNOR Robert B. Smith, accom
panied by the delegation who have
come with him to receive the re
turning volunteers, arrived in this
city yesterday morning from the north.
The Governor himself went to the Occi
dental, but the rest of the party split up
and are registered at the different host
elries around town.
When seen yesterday in his apartments
the Governor said that while no definite
plans had been made for the entertain
ment of the soldiers when they should
get back to their home State, yet money
was being rapidly collected, and by the
time the troops are mustered out things
will be in suoh a shape as to assure them
a reception in every way worthy of their
great achievements.
If there Is enough money collected, and
the Governor is certain there will be. the
troops will be onrrled from this city to
their homes at the expense of the people
of the State whose renown they have
done so much to extend throughout the
entire land.

MARY CROCKER
NOW POSSESSES
HER MILLIONS
An Order Is Made by
Judge Coffey.
FINAL ACCOUNT IS SETTLED
THE HAPPY HEIRESS APPEARS
TO SIGN DOCUMENTS.
Miss Mary Crocker, eldest child of the
late Charles Fred Crocker, formally came
into possession of the vast legacy left her
by her deceased father yesterday. Her
share of the Crocker estate is valued at
something over $4,000,000, and by the order
of Probate Judge Coffey she is now free
to handle this great wealth, as she will
be unhampered by the dictates of a trus
tee or guardian.
Miss Crocker attained her majority on
the 7th of the present month, Charles E.
Green and Henry T. Scott, executors of
the will of Colonel Crocker and guardians
of the person and estate of the young
woman, then prepared their final account.
This was presented in court yesterday
afternoon, the account was accepted atid
the order of distribution made. In this
account It is set forth that during the
months covered, from March to Septem
ber of this year, the receipts have been
$194,217 90; disbursements, $90,917 85, leav
ing a balance on hand of $103,300 03. For
services rendered on behalf of their wards
the guardians asked $10,660 2-3, and the at
torneys, Morrison & Cope, $3333 1-3. This
deduction of $20,000 from the account re
duced the balance to $53,300 05. Miss Crock
er, who was present in court and appar
ently happy that she was now legally
qualified to care for her own interests,
took her pen and entered at the head or
the account that she had examined it
carefully and had no suggestions to offer.
Judge Coffey then ordered the account
settled.
The strong box containing the Crocker
securities, which have for some time been
in the vaults of the Crocker-Woolworth
National Bank, wus then placed on the
bench and opened in the presence of
Judge Coffey. This box contains 649 1-20
shares of the Southern Pacific Company,
valued at $16,226 26; 437 shares Southern
Pacific Railroad Company, valued at $3800;
250 shares Oakland Water Front Com
pany, valued at $3000; 527 shares Rocky
Mountain Coal and Iron Company, valued
at $26,300; 100 shares Wells, Fargo & Co.,
valued at J10.500; 20,340 shares Market
Street Railway Company, valued at $966,
--150; 12&6 shares Crocker-Woolworth Na
tional Bank, valued at $231,480; 19.999
shares Crocker Estate Company, valued
at $3,999,800; 100 shares Union Iron Works,
valued at $10,000; 100 shares Promontory
Ranch Company, valued at $10,0o0; United
States Government 4 per cent bonds, val
ued at $275,000. Total worth of securities,
$5,567,306 25.
Miss Crocker's division of the securities
were selected, shown to her and then set
apart from the rest In the strong box. It
was again sealed up, and then the heavy
burden was taken back to the bank. Aftxr
the ceremony had been concluded Miss
Crocker, accompanied by her attorneys,
left the court.
In about six years from now this cere
mony will be repeated. Charles Temple
ton Crocker is now 15 years of age, and
his sister, Jennie Adeline Crocker, is 12.
Hence they will come of age within a few
months of each other, and it is very prob
able that one ceremony will suffice for the
distribution of their securities to them.
In the meantime their estate will greatly
Increase in value, and when they come
into possession of their estates they will
be, like their sister, numbered among the
richest young people in the United States.
